Delegation of Work: Absolute vs. Restricted Permission in Ijara
Ruling on delegation in ijara contracts when permission is absolute or restricted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and restricted permission in ijara contracts and apply the Hanafi ruling on whether the worker may delegate.

Core Idea
Absolute permission allows delegation; restriction to one's own hand prohibits it.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a hirer gives an artisan absolute permission to perform a task, the worker may use or hire others to complete the work. However, if the hirer explicitly restricts the work to the worker's own hand, delegation is not allowed. This lesson explains the rule and the reasoning behind it, using analogies from debt and hiring specific animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of permission
Determine whether the hirer gave absolute permission (e.g., 'sew this cloth for a dirham') or restricted it to the worker's hand (e.g., 'sew it with your own hand').
2
Apply the rule for absolute permission
If permission is absolute, the worker may delegate or hire another person to perform the work. The obligation is to achieve the outcome, not to labour personally.
The debt analogy: a debt can be paid by the debtor or by a third party.
3
Apply the rule for restricted permission
If the work is restricted to the worker's hand, delegation is prohibited, even to the worker's own slave or hireling. Personal labour is the subject matter of the contract.
The animal hire analogy: a specific animal hired for transport cannot be substituted.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The key distinction is whether the contract specifies personal performance (restricted) or merely the completion of a task (absolute). The ruling follows the general Hanafi principle that contracts are interpreted according to the intention of the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن أطلق له العمل أن يستعمل غيره ، فإن قيد بيده فلا. [٤] قوله : ولمن أطلق ... الخ ; يعني وكل صانع أطلق المستأجر له العمل، فله أن يستعمل ويستأجر غيره، كما إذا أمره أن يخيط هذا الثوب بدرهم، فاللازم عليه العمل، سواء أوفاه بنفسه أو باستعانة غيره، كالمأمور بقضاء الدين، فإنّ الإيفاء يحصل من المديون، وبالتبرع من غيره. وإن قيد العمل بيده بأن قال : خِطْهُ بيدك، فليس له أن يستعمل غيره ولو غلامه أو أجيره ; لأنّ عمله يكون هو المعقود عليه، وإذا استأجر دابة بعينها للحمل فليس للمؤجر أن يُسَلِّم غيرها.
And for the one for whom the work is given absolutely, he may use someone else; if it is restricted to his hand, he may not. [4] He said: 'And for the one for whom...' meaning every artisan to whom the hirer gives absolute permission for the work, he may use and hire another, like if he ordered him to sew this cloth for a dirham, then the work is obligatory upon him, whether he fulfills it himself or with the help of another, like the one ordered to pay a debt: fulfillment is achieved by the debtor or by donation from another. If he restricts the work to his hand by saying 'sew it with your hand', then he may not use another, even his slave or hireling, because his labor is the subject matter of the contract. And if he hires a specific animal for transport, the lessor may not deliver another.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage presents a rule about delegation in hired work. The base text states: 'Wa liman uṭliqa lahu al-ʿamalu an yastaʿmila ghayrahu, fa-in qayyida biyadihi falā.' That is, if the hirer gives absolute permission for the task (e.g., 'sew this cloth for a dirham' without specifying who does it), the worker may delegate the work to another person or hire someone else to do it. The reason is that the obligation is to complete the work, and it can be fulfilled by the worker himself or by someone he engages. The example is given of someone ordered to pay a debt: the debt can be paid by the debtor or by a third party. However, if the hirer explicitly restricts the work to the worker's own hand (e.g., 'sew it with your hand'), then delegation is not allowed, even to the worker's own slave or hired assistant. This is because the worker's personal labor is the subject matter of the contract; delivering the product of another's labor would be akin to delivering a different subject matter. The analogy is made with hiring a specific animal for transport: the lessor cannot substitute another animal because the contract specifies a particular object.

Key Terms
أطلق له العمل (uṭliqa lahu al-ʿamalu): The hirer gives absolute permission for the work without specifying who performs it.
قيد بيده (qayyada biyadihi): The hirer restricts the work to the worker's own hand, making personal performance obligatory.
استعمل غيره (yastaʿmilu ghayrahu): The worker uses another person to perform the work, either as a free helper or a hired substitute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hirer says to a tailor, 'Sew this cloth for a dirham with your own hand,' can the tailor have his apprentice sew it instead?
A) Yes, because the tailor is ultimately responsible for the work.
B) No, because the work is restricted to the tailor's own hand.
C) Yes, as long as the hirer gets the cloth back sewn.
D) No, but he can hire another tailor to do it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, delegation in ijara contracts is permitted unless the hirer explicitly restricts the work to the worker's personal hand.

Retention Rights for Service Providers: Boatman, Washer, and Returner of Runaway
Ruling on retention rights with qiyas proof and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on retention rights for the boatman, washer, and returner of a runaway slave, and explain the qiyas-based proof and Zufar's opposing view.

Core Idea
Only the returner of a runaway may retain; boatman and washer cannot, because their work does not create new value.

In ijarah, a worker may sometimes withhold the subject matter until paid. However, Hanafi law distinguishes between workers whose work saves from destruction (like returning a runaway) and those whose work merely provides a service without creating new value (like boatmen and washers). This lesson examines the basis for this distinction and the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parties and their rulings
From the passage, state the retention ruling for: (1) boatman, (2) washer, (3) returner of runaway. Note the contrast.
Boatman and washer: no right; returner: right of retention.
2
Analyze the proof for the returner's retention
The passage uses an analogy (qiyas): the runaway was on the verge of destruction, so returning him is like giving life, which is akin to selling him. Identify the asl (original case), far' (new case), and illa (effective cause).
Asl = seller's right of retention; Far' = returner's retention; Illa = creation of value or rescue from destruction.
3
Understand the boatman/washer exception
The text states that boatman and washer have no retention. Reason: their work does not create a new value or save from destruction. Note the nuance: if the washer's work is for beautification (not mere purification), retention is allowed.
Washing for beautification creates new value (whiteness) similar to a sale.
4
Examine Zufar's opposing view
Imam Zufar holds that even the returner has no right of retention, whether his work left a trace or not. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
Zufar's view: returner cannot withhold the slave at all.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Given the principles, determine retention rights for other workers (e.g., a tailor who makes a garment vs. a repairer who fixes a broken item). Use the criterion of 'creating new value' or 'saving from destruction'.
Tailor creates new value → retention; repairer may not if only restoring original state.

[1] "والملاح" وغاسل النوب) لا حبس له ، بخلافِ راد الابق) ، فإنَّ الآبق كان على شرف الهلاك، فكأنه أحياه، وباع منه بالجعل، وعند زفر ه ليس له حق الحبس سواء كان لعمله أثر في العين أو لا.

The boatman and the clothes washer have no right of retention, unlike the returner of a runaway slave. And whoever is given absolute freedom to do the work may employ someone else; if it is restricted to his own hand, then not.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from the chapter on ijarah. First: the boatman (ships captain) and the person who washes clothes for purification (not beautification) cannot withhold the item for their wage. This is contrasted with the returner of a runaway slave, who may retain the slave until paid because his act saves the slave from destruction, resembling a sale. Imam Zufar disagrees, holding no retention for the returner either. Second: if a hirer gives the worker unrestricted permission to do the work, the worker may hire others or have them assist. However, if the hirer specifies 'with your own hand,' then the worker cannot delegate even to his own employee or slave. This is like hiring a specific animal for transport.

Key Terms
ملاح (mallāh): Boatman or ship captain who operates a vessel.
غاسل الثوب (ghāsil al-thawb): One who washes clothes for purification (not beautification).
راد الآبق (rādd al-ābiq): One who returns a runaway slave to its owner.
حبس (habs): Right to withhold the subject matter until payment is received.
جعل (ju‘l): Reward or fee promised for a specific service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the returner's work saves the slave from destruction, resembling a sale.
B) Because the returner's work leaves a visible trace on the slave.
C) Because the boatman and washer are not considered 'workers' in ijarah.
D) Because the returner is entitled to a ju‘l (reward) by default.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, retention (habs) is only permitted for workers whose labor creates new value or rescues property from destruction.

Delegation of Work in Ijarah: When the Worker May Hire Others
Ruling on delegation of work in ijarah with example from tailoring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on delegation of work: if the hirer gives absolute permission, the worker may employ others; if restricted to his own hand, he may not.

Core Idea
Absolute permission implies freedom to delegate; explicit restriction binds the worker to personal performance.

In an ijarah contract, the worker is usually required to perform the work personally. However, if the hirer does not specify that the work must be done by the worker's own hand, the general principle is that the worker may delegate the task to another, just as one authorized to pay a debt may use someone else's money. This lesson clarifies the conditions for delegation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default rule
If the hirer says 'do this work' without specifying 'by your own hand,' the worker may employ someone else to do it. This is because the obligation is to produce the result, not to perform personally.
Analogy: one ordered to pay a debt may use another person's funds.
2
Identify the restriction
If the hirer explicitly says 'do it with your own hand' or similar, then the worker cannot delegate, even to his own employee or slave.
The contract then is for personal service.
3
Apply to different scenarios
For example, if A says to B 'sew this cloth for a dirham,' B may have someone else sew it. But if A says 'sew it with your own hand,' B must do it personally.
This is from the text: 'كما إذا أمره أن يخيطه بيده'.
4
Note the nuance with transport
The commentary adds that if someone hires a 'camel driver' (jammāl), the term is specific and delegation is not allowed, but if he says 'carry this load,' it is general and delegation is permissible.
This shows that wording matters.
5
Formulate the ruling in a principle
Derive: In ijarah, the worker is not obligated to perform personally unless the contract explicitly requires personal performance (muta'ayyin).
This is a general Hanafi principle.

Source Text

( ولمن أطلق له العملُ أن يستعمل غيره ، فإن قيد بيده فلا) ، كما إذا أمرَهُ أن يخيطه بيده. وما قررناه في المتن هو أولى؛ لأنَّ الحمل يجوز أن يقع على الظهر وعلى الدابة فيكون أعم (من) لفظ الجمال بالجيم. 11 ] قوله : والملاح ؛ بالتشديد السفان، وهو الذي يجري السفينة في البحر.
[٢] قوله : وغاسل الثوب ؛ أي لتطهيره لا لتحسينه، فإنّه إذا كان يغسل لتحسينه فله حق الحبس ، قال في «البحر»: أطلقته فشمل أو إذا لم يكن لعمله إلا إزالة الدرن بالغسل فقط على الأصح ؛ لأنَّ البياض كان مستتراً ، وقد ظهر بفعله، فكأنه أحدثه فيه.
انتهى.
[٣]قوله : بخلاف راد الآبق ؛ يعني بخلافِ راد الآبق فإنّه يحبسه على الجعل، وإن لم يكن لعمله أثر ؛ لأنه كان على شرف الزوال والهلاك، فأحياه بالرد ، فكأنه باعه، فكان له حق الحبس.
[٤] قوله : ولمن أطلق ... الخ ؛ يعني وكل صانع أطلق المستأجر له العمل، فله أن يستعمل ويستأجر غيره، كما إذا أمره أن يخيط هذا الثوب بدرهم، فاللازم عليه العمل، سواء أوفاه بنفسه أو باستعانة غيره، كالمأمور بقضاء الدين، فإنّ الإيفاء يحصل من المديون، وبالتبرع من غيره. وإن قيد العمل بيده بأن قال : خِطْهُ بيدك، فليس له أن يستعمل غيره ولو غلامه أو أجيره ؛ لأنّ عمله يكون هو المعقود عليه، وإذا استأجر دابة بعينها للحمل فليس للمؤجر أن يُسَلِّم غيرها.
The boatman and the clothes washer have no right of retention, unlike the returner of a runaway slave. And whoever is given absolute freedom to do the work may employ someone else; if it is restricted to his own hand, then not.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from the chapter on ijarah. First: the boatman (ships captain) and the person who washes clothes for purification (not beautification) cannot withhold the item for their wage. This is contrasted with the returner of a runaway slave, who may retain the slave until paid because his act saves the slave from destruction, resembling a sale. Imam Zufar disagrees, holding no retention for the returner either. Second: if a hirer gives the worker unrestricted permission to do the work, the worker may hire others or have them assist. However, if the hirer specifies 'with your own hand,' then the worker cannot delegate even to his own employee or slave. This is like hiring a specific animal for transport.

Key Terms
أطلق له العمل (aṭlaqa lahu al-‘amal): Gave absolute freedom to do the work without restriction.
قيّد بيده (qayyada bi-yadihi): Restricted the work to the worker's own hand.
حمل (ḥaml): Carrying or transport, a general term.
جمال (jammāl): Camel driver, a specific profession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hirer says to a worker: 'Paint my house for $200.' Nothing else is said. Can the worker hire another painter to do the job?
A) Yes, because the contract is for the result, not personal performance.
B) No, because painting requires the contracted worker's skill.
C) Yes, but only if the new painter charges less.
D) No, because delegation is not allowed in house painting.

Key Takeaway
In ijarah, delegation is permissible unless the contract explicitly requires personal performance.

Proportionate Wage for the Ajir When Some of the Contracted Subject Matter Is Fulfilled
Explains wage entitlement for ajir when some family members die

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wage entitlement when an ajir fulfills only part of the contracted service and apply the principle of proportionate payment.

Core Idea
Wage is earned proportionally to the fulfilled part of the contract, not lost entirely if some items perish.

This passage from the chapter on Ijarah addresses a common scenario: if a hired person (ajir) to bring a family finds some members dead, is he entitled to any wage? The Hanafi ruling is clear: payment follows fulfillment. The commentary also draws an analogy with a carriage contract to illustrate how the wording of the contract affects entitlement. Let's break down the logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract's Subject Matter
Determine exactly what was contracted. Was it to bring a specific number of family members (known number) or a general group (unknown number)? This determines the basis for wage calculation.
If the number is known, the wage is tied to each individual; if unknown, the wage is for the entire effort.
2
Apply the Proportion Principle
For a known number: wage is divided by the number of members; the ajir receives wage for those actually brought. For an unknown number: the ajir receives full wage because the contract was not itemized.
This is based on the rule 'compensation is due for the fulfilled part of the contract'.
3
Distinguish the Carriage Case
Examine the analogous case of hiring to carry wheat. The wording determines if the journey is part of the contract. If the contract includes 'from the city to carry from the village', the journey is part and half wage is due for going alone. If only 'to carry from the village', nothing is due for the journey.
This illustrates that the contract's explicit terms define what constitutes fulfillment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأجير المجيء بعياله إن مات بعضُهم، وجاءَ بمن بقي أجرة بحسابه. 
[1) قوله : ولأجير المجيء....الخ ; يعني من استأجر رجلاً ليذهب إلى الأجرة مثلاً فيجيء بعياله ، فوجد بعضهم ميتاً ، فأتى بمن بقي من العيال فللأ أجره بحسابه ; لأنه جير أوفى بعض المعقود عليه، فيستحق العوض بقدر ما أوفى، وبطل بقدر ما لم يوف، وهذا إذا كان عدد عياله معلوماً، حتى يكون الأجر مقابلاً بجملتهم، وأما إذا كانوا غير معلومين فالأجير يستحق جميع الأجرة. صرح به العيني)، والزيلعي)، وغيرهما. وإذا استأجر في المصر مثلاً ليحمل الحنطة من القرية، فذهب الأجير فلم يجد الحنطة، فعاد فإن كان قال : استأجرت منك من المصر حتى أحمل الحنطة من القرية، يجب نصف الأجر بالذهاب. وإن قال : استأجرتُ منك حتى أحمل من القرية لا يجب شيء ; لأنَّ الإجارة كانت بشرط الحمل لا غير وفي الصورة الأولى كان العقد على شيئين الذهاب إلى ذلك الموضع والحمل منه، وقد ذهب إليه، فاستوفي بعض المعقود عليه، فيجب الأجر بحصته. ذكره في «النهاية» نقلاً عن الفضلي.
And for the hireling who is to bring his family: if some of them die and he brings those who remain, he receives the wage proportionally. Commentary: meaning one who hires a man to go to the village and bring his family, and he finds some dead, he brings the remaining; he gets wage proportionally because he fulfilled part of the contract; he is entitled to compensation according to what he fulfilled, and void according to what he did not fulfill. This is if the number of his family is known, so that the wage corresponds to all of them. But if they are unknown, the hireling receives all the wage, as stated by al-Ayni, al-Zaylai, and others. And if one hires in the city to carry wheat from the village, and the hireling goes and does not find the wheat and returns: if he said 'I hired you from the city to carry the wheat from the village', half the wage is due for the journey. If he said 'I hired you to carry from the village', nothing is due because the contract was conditional on carriage only, not on going. In the first case, the contract was on two things: going to that place and carrying from it, and he went there, so he fulfilled part of the contract, thus wage proportional to it.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Ijarah presents a ruling about an ajir (hireling) hired to bring someone's family. The commentary explains that if some family members die before delivery, the ajir is entitled to wage proportional to the number brought, because he fulfilled part of the contract. If the number of family members is unknown at the time of contract, the presumption is that the wage was intended for the entire effort, so he receives full wage. An analogous case is then given: hiring to carry wheat from a village. If the contract specifies 'from the city to carry from the village', the ajir gets half wage for the journey when no wheat is found. If it only says 'to carry from the village', nothing is due because the contract was only for carriage, not for the journey. This illustrates the principle that wage entitlement follows fulfillment of the contracted subject matter.

Key Terms
الأجير (al-ajir): A person hired to perform a service for a wage.
المعقود عليه (al-ma'qud alayh): The subject matter of the contract; what the contract is about.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an ajir is hired to bring a family of 10, but 3 die before he arrives, how much wage is he entitled to?
A) Full wage because he made the journey
B) Wage for 7/10 because he fulfilled part of the contract
C) Nothing because the contract's purpose was defeated
D) Half wage regardless

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the wage is tied to the actual fulfillment of the contracted subject matter, not to the effort.

Ruling on Ajir Hired to Carry a Letter or Food: Death of Recipient
Examines wage entitlement when addressee dies, with disagreement among Hanafi imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on wage entitlement for a carrier who returns an undelivered item due to the recipient's death, and identify which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The contract's subject matter is delivery, not travel; returning defeats the work so no wage is due.

This passage examines a practical scenario: a hired messenger sets out to deliver a letter or food, but finds the recipient dead. Should he still be paid? The Hanafi imams disagree based on how they define the subject matter of the contract. Understanding this dispute reveals the school's method in analyzing 'ijara contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal issue
An ajir is hired to carry a letter (qitt) or food (zad) to Zaid. He travels, finds Zaid dead, and returns the item. Does he deserve the wage?
The scenario assumes the contract specifies delivery to Zaid personally.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They hold that no wage is due because the subject matter (ma'qud alayh) is the delivery itself. Returning the item undoes the work.
The wage is for the transport as a means to the purpose; if the purpose is defeated, no wage.
3
State the position of Imam Muhammad
Muhammad holds that wage is due for the journey because the wage is for the hardship of travel, which was fulfilled.
He considers the ma'qud alayh to be traversing distance, not the delivery outcome.
4
Note the partial distinction of Zufar
Zufar distinguishes: for food, wage is due because carriage has weight and hardship; for a letter, no wage because the item is light.
The majority rejects this distinction, arguing the wage is for transport in both cases.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and the reasoning
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no wage if the item is returned. The contract's purpose is delivery, and returning it defeats the contract, like a tailor undoing his stitching.
This is supported by the analogy to a tailor who undoes his work after finishing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحامل قط أو زاد إلى زيد بأجر إن رده لموته.
قوله : وحامل ...الخ ; يعني من استأجر رجلاً ليذهب بكتابه أو بطعامه إلى زيد في البصرة مثلاً ، فذهب إليها فوجده ميتاً ، فرد ذلك الكتاب أو الطعام إلى المستأجر لموت زيد فلا أجر له. قوله : لموته ; ولو وجده غائباً فهو كما لو وجده ميتاً ; لتعذر الوصول إليه، ولو ترك الكتاب هناك ليوصل إليه أو إلى ورثته ، فله الأجر في الذهاب ; لأنه أتى بما في وسعه، وكذا لو استأجر رسولاً ليبلغ رسالته إلى فلان بغداد، فلم يجد فلاناً، وعاد فله لا شيء له هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وعند محمد له أجر الذهاب.
في القط: أي الكتاب ، وفي الزاد لا شيء له اتفاقا حيث نقض عمله بالرد. الأجر; لأن الأجر بقطع المسافة ; لأنه في وسعه، وأما الإسماع فليس في وسعه فلا يقابله الأجر. ذكره السمني والزيلعي وغيرهما.
قوله : هذا عند...الخ ; لهما إنّ المعقود عليه هو نقل الكتاب إذ هو الأمر المقصود بين الناس; لأن فيه تعظيم المكتوب إليه ، وصلة الرحم، أو وسيلة إلى المقصود، وهو العلم في الكتاب، فإذا رده فقد نقضه، أما حقيقة فظاهر، وأما اعتبارا ; فلأن ترك الكتاب ثمة مفيد، فإنه قد يصل إلى ورثته فينتفعون به ، فإذا فات المقصود فقد صار الأخير ناقضا للعمل، فلا يستحق الأجر.
قوله : وعند محمد له أجر الذهاب ; لأنه أوفى في بعض المعقود عليه دون البعض، فيستحق الأجر بقدر ما أوفى، وهذا لأن المعقود عليه هاهنا قطع المسافة ; لأن الأجر مقابل به لما فيه من المشقة دون نقل الكتاب لخفة مؤنته.
قوله : أي الكتاب ; قال في المصباح المنير : القط : الكتاب، والجمع قطوط، مثل حمل وحمول. انتهى.
قوله : اتفاقا ; أي بين الإمام وصاحبيه، وقال زفر : له الأجر في الطعام ; لأن الأجر يقابل حمل الطعام ، وقد وفى بالمشروط بخلاف نقل الكتاب ; فلانه لا أجر فيه، فإنه لا يقابل الحمل، فإنه لا مؤنة. والجواب أن الأجر مقابل بالنقل في هاتين الصورتين ; لأنه وسيلة إلى المقصود، وهو وضع الطعام في البصرة مثلا، وعلم المكتوب إليه بما في الكتاب فإذا رده فقد نقض المعقود عليه فلا يستحق الأجر، وهذا كما إذا نقض الخياط الخياطة بعد الفراغ فلا يستحق الأجر.
And a carrier of a letter or food to Zaid for hire, if he returns it due to his death: commentary: meaning one who hires a man to go with his book or his food to Zaid in Basra, he goes and finds him dead, so he returns the book or food to the hirer; he has no wage. And if he finds him absent, it is like finding him dead due to inability to reach him. If he leaves the book there to be delivered to him or his heirs, he has wage for the journey because he did what he could. Likewise, if one hires a messenger to deliver his message to someone in Baghdad, and he does not find him and returns, he gets nothing according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; according to Muhammad, he gets wage for the journey. Regarding the letter: it means the book; regarding food: he gets nothing by agreement because his work was undone by the return. The wage is for traversing the distance because that is within his capacity; but making heard is not within his capacity so no wage for it. This is stated by al-Samani and al-Zaylai. Explanation: For the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the subject matter of the contract is the transport of the book since that is the intended purpose: showing respect, maintaining kinship, or means to the knowledge in the book. So if he returns it, he undoes the work. Actually and effectively: leaving the book would be beneficial as it may reach heirs. If the purpose is defeated, he becomes a undoer of the work, so he deserves no wage. According to Muhammad, he gets wage for the journey because he fulfilled part of the contract; the subject matter here is traversing distance as the wage is for the hardship, not for transport of the book due to its lightness. He said: 'al-qitt' means the book, plural qutut. He said: 'by agreement' means between the Imam (Abu Hanifa) and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Zufar said: he gets wage for the food because the wage is for carrying food and he fulfilled the condition, unlike the book; for the book he gets nothing. The answer: the wage is for transport in both cases because it is a means to the purpose: placing the food in Basra and informing the addressee of the book's content. If he returns it, he undoes the contract and does not deserve wage, like a tailor who undoes the stitching after finishing.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding an ajir hired to deliver a letter (qitt) or food (zad) to a specific person (Zaid). If Zaid dies and the carrier returns the item, the majority of the school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rules that no wage is due because the subject matter of the contract (al-ma'qud alayh) is the delivery and communication, not merely the journey. Returning the item defeats the purpose. However, Imam Muhammad holds that the wage is for the journey itself, as the difficulty lies in traveling; the transport of the item is incidental. Therefore, the carrier deserves wage for the traveled distance. Zufar adds nuance: for food, wage is due because the carriage has weight and hardship; for a letter, nothing. The commentator clarifies that in both cases, the wage is for the transport as a means to the purpose; if the purpose is undone by returning, no wage is due, analogous to a tailor who undoes his sewing. This ikhtilaf illustrates the Hanafi method of determining the 'illa (effective cause) in ijarah contracts.

Key Terms
القط (al-qitt): A letter or written document.
الزاد (al-zad): Provision, food supplies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, when does a hired carrier who returns a letter due to the recipient's death get no wage?
A) Only if the letter is returned, but not if food is returned.
B) Because the subject matter is delivery, and returning it undoes the work.
C) Because the carrier did not travel the entire distance.
D) Because the carrier did not inform the hirer first.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the subject matter (ma'qud alayh) is decisive; if the carrier returns the item, the purpose is defeated and no wage is due, even if travel occurred.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Wage When Delivery Is Refused
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wage for rejected delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on wage entitlement for a messenger when the delivered item is refused, and explain how each determines the subject matter of the contract.

Core Idea
Wage entitlement hinges on the subject matter: transport of the item or the journey itself.

When a hired messenger delivers a book or food but the recipient refuses to accept it, is the messenger entitled to any wage? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. The answer depends on what the contract's subject matter (al-ma'qud 'alayh) is: the physical transport of the item or the act of traveling. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of the classical Hanafi imams and reveals the practical consequences of their differing definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the differing opinions
The passage presents two main positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that no wage is due if the item is rejected, while Muhammad holds that the messenger deserves the wage for the outward journey. Note also Zufar's view on the food case.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; all opinions are valid within the school.
2
Determine each side's definition of the subject matter
For Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the subject matter is transporting the book (or food) because that is the customary purpose. For Muhammad, the subject matter is the journey itself (cutting the distance) because the wage compensates for hardship.
The key is what the parties intended and what the wage is exchanged for.
3
See how the definition leads to the ruling
If the subject matter is transport of the item, rejecting it undoes the contract and no wage is due. If the subject matter is the journey, then performing the journey partially (outward trip) entitles the messenger to a proportional wage.
The ruling follows from the definition of the contract's object.
4
Note the exception for food (al-zad) and Zufar's view
The text says that for food, the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that no wage is due if rejected. Zufar dissents, arguing the wage is for carrying food, which was done. The consensus answer is that the transport is a means to a purpose that was undone.
This shows that even within the school, analogical reasoning can lead to different conclusions.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference matters in practice: under Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf the messenger gets nothing; under Muhammad he gets a portion (typically half) for the outward journey.
This affects how courts or muftis decide cases of rejected delivery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شيء له لا شيء له، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وعند محمد له أجر الذهاب. في القط: أي الكتاب، وفي الزاد لا شيء له اتفاقا حيث نقض عمله بالرد. الأجر؛ لأن الأجر بقطع المسافة؛ لأنه في وسعه، وأما الإسماع فليس في وسعه فلا يقابله الأجر. ذكره السمني والزيلعي وغيرهما. قوله: هذا عند...الخ؛ لهما إن المعقود عليه هو نقل الكتاب إذ هو الأمر المقصود بين الناس؛ لأن فيه تعظيم المكتوب إليه، وصلة الرحم، أو وسيلة إلى المقصود، وهو العلم في الكتاب، فإذا ردّه فقد نقضه، أما حقيقة فظاهر، وأما اعتبارا؛ فلأن ترك الكتاب ثمة مفيد، فإنه قد يصل إلى ورثته فينتفعون به، فإذا فات المقصود فقد صار الأخير ناقضا للعمل، فلا يستحق الأجر. قوله: وعند محمد له أجر الذهاب؛ لأنه أوفى في بعض المعقود عليه دون البعض، فيستحق الأجر بقدر ما أوفى، وهذا لأن المعقود عليه هاهنا قطع المسافة؛ لأن الأجر مقابل به لما فيه من المشقة دون نقل الكتاب لخفة مؤنته. قوله: أي الكتاب؛ قال في المصباح المنير: القط: الكتاب، والجمع قطوط، مثل حمل وحمول. انتهى. قوله: اتفاقا؛ أي بين الإمام وصاحبيه، وقال زفر: له الأجر في الطعام؛ لأن الأجر يقابل حمل الطعام، وقد وفى بالمشروط بخلاف نقل الكتاب؛ فلانه لا أجر فيه، فإنه لا يقابل الحمل، فإنه لا مؤنة. والجواب أن الأجر مقابل بالنقل في هاتين الصورتين؛ لأنه وسيلة إلى المقصود، وهو وضع الطعام في البصرة مثلا، وعلم المكتوب إليه بما في الكتاب فإذا ردّه فقد نقض المعقود عليه فلا يستحق الأجر، وهذا كما إذا نقض الخياط الخياطة بعد الفراغ فلا يستحق الأجر.
He gets nothing, he gets nothing. This according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and according to Muhammad he has the wage for the going. In 'al-qatt': meaning the book, and in 'al-zad' he gets nothing by agreement where his work is undone by the rejection. The wage because the wage is for cutting the distance, because it is within his capacity; as for making hear, it is not within his capacity so no wage is given for it. Al-Samani and al-Zayla'i and others mentioned it. His statement: 'this according to...etc.'; for them (the two) the subject matter of the contract is transporting the book, since it is the intended matter among people, because in it is honoring the addressee, maintaining family ties, or a means to the intended goal, which is knowledge in the book. So if he rejects it, he has undone it. In reality it is apparent, and in consideration, because leaving the book there is beneficial, as it may reach his heirs and they benefit from it. So if the purpose is lost, the later one becomes an undoer of the work, so he does not deserve the wage. His statement: 'and according to Muhammad, he has the wage for the going', because he fulfilled part of the subject matter without the other, so he deserves wage according to what he fulfilled. And this because the subject matter here is cutting the distance, because the wage is in exchange for the hardship it contains, not for transporting the book due to its slight burden. His statement: 'meaning the book'; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: al-qatt is the book, plural qutut, like haml and humul. End. His statement: 'by agreement'; meaning between the Imam and his two companions, and Zufar said: he has the wage for food, because the wage is in exchange for carrying the food, and he fulfilled the condition, unlike transporting the book, because he has no wage in it, as it does not correspond to carrying, as there is no burden. The answer is that the wage is in exchange for transport in both these cases, because it is a means to the purpose, which is placing the food in Basra for example, and informing the addressee of what is in the book. So if he rejects it, he has undone the subject matter, so he does not deserve the wage, just like if the tailor undoes the sewing after finishing, he does not deserve the wage.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the entitlement to wage in a contract of hire (ijara) where a messenger is hired to deliver a book, but the recipient refuses to accept it (the book is returned). The disagreement centers on what constitutes the subject matter of the contract (al-ma'qud 'alayh). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the subject matter is the transport of the book itself, as it is the intended service that honors the recipient, maintains ties, or conveys knowledge. If the book is rejected, the purpose is lost, and the messenger has effectively undone his work, so he deserves no wage. Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, holds that the subject matter is merely the act of traversing the distance, since the wage is compensation for the hardship of the journey, not for the book's transport which involves little burden. Since the messenger performed the journey (partially fulfilled the subject matter), he deserves the wage for the going trip. The text further clarifies that in the case of delivering food provisions (al-zad), all three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) agree that no wage is due if the food is rejected, because the subject matter (transport of food) is undone. Zufar disputes this, arguing that like the book case, the wage should correspond to the physical transport, but the consensus view uses the same reasoning: the transport is a means to the goal, and once rejected, the contract is undone. This reasoning is likened to a tailor who unwittingly undoes his stitching—he loses entitlement to the wage. The passage underscores the Hanafi method of determining the subject matter of a contract based on customary purpose (urf) and the effective cause of the wage (hardship).

Key Terms
الأجر (al-ajr): The wage or compensation due to the hired party (messenger) for performing the contracted service.
المعقود عليه (al-ma'qud 'alayh): The subject matter of the contract; what the parties have agreed upon as the object of the hire.
القط (al-qatt): A book (kitab); also a written document or letter.
الزاد (al-zad): Provisions, especially food supplies for a journey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why does Muhammad hold that the messenger deserves a wage for the outward journey?
A) Because the messenger fully transported the book and the recipient's refusal is irrelevant.
B) Because the subject matter is the journey itself, and the messenger performed a part of it.
C) Because the contract is for hire of services, and any work done must be paid.
D) Because the messenger incurred the cost of travel.

Key Takeaway
The wage entitlement in a rejected delivery contract depends on the definition of the subject matter: transport of the item (Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf) vs. the journey itself (Muhammad), with the majority later preferring the former view.

Renting Without Specifying Use: The Power of Custom in Hanafi Ijarah
Ruling on validity of rental without specifying work, based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan based on custom ('urf) to validate rental contracts without specified use, and distinguish permissible from impermissible tenant activities.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) supplies the missing term in a rental contract, making it valid despite unspecified use.

One of the most common real estate transactions is renting a property without explicitly stating its purpose. Hanafi fiqh, using the tool of istihsan, upholds such contracts by referring to the customary use of the property. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the classical commentary and shows how custom functions as a binding term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the ruling: it is valid (sahih) to rent a house or shop without specifying what work will be done. The contract is not void despite the lack of explicit use.
This is the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the opposing view (qiyas)
By strict analogy, the rental would be invalid because the purpose is unknown (land requires specification of crop, clothes require specification of wearer). The uncertainty (gharar) would void the contract.
This is the qiyas objection.
3
Grasp the istihsan reasoning
Hanafi jurists apply istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom ('urf). Since the customary use of a house or shop is dwelling/shelter, the contract is understood to be for that purpose – custom is treated as if it were a stipulated condition.
The basis: 'al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan'.
4
Distinguish from other contracts
Contrast with renting land or clothes. Land varies by crop, clothes vary by wearer; there is no single customary use, so specification is required. For houses and shops, the custom is uniform (dwelling or storage), so specification is not needed.
This distinction is crucial.
5
Apply the principle to permissible activities
The tenant may do any work that does not weaken the building. Examples: perform ablution, bathe, wash clothes, break firewood, tie animals in a customary place, house whomever they wish. However, work causing structural harm (like laundering, blacksmithing, grinding) is prohibited.
Custom also determines limits (e.g., in Bukhara, animals are not allowed due to narrow houses).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يجوز من الإجارة وما لا يجوز
وصح استئجار دار أو دُكّان بلا ذكر ما يعمل فيه، وله كل عمل سوى موهن البناء كالقصارة.
[1] قوله : وصح استئجار ...الخ ؛ القياس أن لا يصح؛ لأن المقصود من الدار والدكان هو الانتفاع ، وهو قد يكون بالسكني، وقد يكون بوضع الأمتعة، فينبغي أن لا يجوز ما لم يبين العمل كالأراضي للزراعة والثياب للبس، ووجه الاستحسان ما ذكره الشارح بقوله : فإن العمل المتعارف...الخ.
وحاصله : إنَّ العمل المتعارف فيها السكني، والمتعارف كالمشروط، فينصرفُ العقد إلى السكني، بخلاف الأرض والثياب، فإنّهما يختلفان باختلاف المزروع واللابس، فلا بد من البيان.
[2] قوله : وله كل عمل ؛ يعني وللمستأجر أن يعمل في الدار والدكان : أي يعمل كل شيء من العمل ؛ لأنها لا تختلف باختلافِ العامل والعمل، فجاز له أن يعمل فيه ما شاء عند الإطلاق، فله أن يتوضأ ويغتسل ويغسل الثياب، ويكسر الحطب المعتاد، ويستنجي بحائطه، ويدق فيه وتداً، ويربط الدواب في موضع معتاد له، ويسكنها من أحبب، سواء كان بإجارة أو غيرها وليس للأجير أن يدخل دابته الدار المستأجرة بعدما سكن المستأجر. وذكر في «النهاية» وغيرها : لا يدخل الدواب في عرفنا ؛ لأنَّ المنازل ببخارا تضيق عن سكنى الناس، فكيف تتسع بإدخال الدواب، وإنما هذا الجواب بناء على عرفهم في الكوفة ، فحاصله : إنَّ العمل مبني على العــرف ، وذكر في «الذخيرة» وغيرها : إنما يكون له ربط الدواب إذا كان فيها موضع معد لذلك ، وأما إذا لم يكن فليس له ذلك.
[3] قوله : سوى موهن البناء ؛ أي سوى عمل يوهن البناء، ويضعفه كالقصارة والحدادة والطحن، فلا يسكن قصاراً ولا حداداً ولا طحانا ؛ لأنه فيه ضرراً ظاهراً، فإنه
Chapter on what is permissible in rental and what is not. And it is valid to rent a house or shop without mentioning what work is to be done in it, and he (the tenant) may do any work except that which weakens the building, like laundering. [1] The author's statement: 'And it is valid to rent...etc.'; by analogy (qiyas) it would not be valid, because the purpose of the house and shop is to benefit, and that may be by dwelling or by placing goods, so it should not be valid unless the work is specified, like land for cultivation and clothes for wearing. The basis for juristic preference (istihsan) is what the commentator mentioned: 'For the customary work ...etc.' Its summary: The customary work in them is dwelling, and the customary is like a condition, so the contract is directed to dwelling, unlike land and clothes, for they vary with the type of crop and wearer, so specification is necessary. [2] The author's statement: 'and he may do any work'; meaning the tenant may do in the house and shop any work; because they do not vary with the worker and the work, so he may do whatever he wishes when unrestricted. Thus he may perform ablution, bathe, wash clothes, break customary firewood, relieve himself against its wall, drive a peg, tie animals in a place customary for that, and house whomever he wishes, whether by rental or otherwise. The hireling may not bring his animal into the rented house after the tenant has moved in. It is mentioned in 'al-Nihayah' and others: Animals are not allowed in our custom, because houses in Bukhara are too narrow for people to live in, so how could they accommodate animals? This answer is based on their custom in Kufa. So the gist is that work is based on custom. It is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhirah' and others: He may tie animals only if there is a place prepared for that; if not, he may not. [3] The author's statement: 'except that which weakens the building'; meaning except any work that weakens and damages the building, like laundering, blacksmithing, and grinding. So he may not house a launderer, blacksmith, or miller, because it causes manifest harm.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqayah, expanding the ruling on renting a house or shop without specifying the use. The commentator begins by stating the ruling: such a rental is valid, and the tenant may do any work except that which weakens the building. He then explains the reasoning: by strict analogy (qiyas), the rental would be invalid because the purpose of the property is unspecified. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom ('urf). Since the customary use of a house or shop is dwelling, the contract is understood to be for dwelling, just as if it had been stipulated. This is contrasted with land (which varies by crop) and clothes (which vary by wearer), where specification is required. The commentator then details permissible activities: anything that does not weaken the building, including performing ablution, bathing, washing clothes, breaking firewood, even tying animals if that is customary. However, this is subject to local custom; for example, in Bukhara, houses are too small for animals. Finally, he explains 'weakening the building' with examples like laundering, blacksmithing, and grinding—professions that cause structural harm. The tenant cannot rent to such tradesmen. This ruling relies on the Hanafi principle that custom has the force of a condition ('al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan).

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract for usufruct in exchange for compensation; rental or hire.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference abandoning strict analogy for a more equitable ruling based on custom, necessity, or public good.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom or common practice that determines contractual terms in the absence of explicit stipulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, a tenant who rents a house without specifying the use may do all of the following EXCEPT:
A) Perform ablution and bathe
B) Break firewood
C) Use it as a blacksmith workshop
D) Tie animals if there is a prepared place

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, custom ('urf) functions as a stipulated condition, validating the contract and defining the scope of permitted use.

Renting Without Specifying Use: Custom and Istihsan
Ruling with proof via qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on renting a house or shop without specifying use and explain how istihsan based on custom overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
Customary use is like a stipulation: a lease without specification is valid for the customary purpose, and istihsan overrides qiyas.

In this passage, the author addresses a common practical question: if someone rents a house or shop without mentioning what it will be used for, is the lease valid? By strict analogy (qiyas), it would be invalid because the usufruct varies. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom (urf), which treats customary use as an implied condition. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the analogical counter-argument, the role of istihsan, and the limits on the lessee's permitted uses.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the main ruling: renting a house or shop without specifying use is valid (sahih). The default use is residence based on custom.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Recognise the analogical reasoning that would require specification. The usufruct of a house differs by use (storage, residence, etc.), just as land for cultivation requires specifying the crop.
Qiyas would require specification in all cases.
3
Identify the istihsan basis
Istihsan overrides qiyas because custom is like a condition. The customary use in a house is residence, so the contract is directed to that without express mention.
The principle: al-muta‘āraf ka al-mashrūt.
4
Work through the exceptions
The lessee may do any work except 'muwahin al-bina'—work that weakens the building (e.g., fulling, blacksmithing). If such work is done, liability for damage arises.
Custom also defines what is permissible: bringing animals is allowed only if there is a prepared place.
5
Note dispute resolution
If the lessor and lessee dispute whether a weakening use was agreed, the lessor's word is taken, but if both produce evidence, the lessee's evidence prevails because it proves an increase.
This follows the general rule in evidence.

Source Text

وحاصله : إنَّ العمل المتعارف فيها السكني، والمتعارف كالمشروط، فينصرفُ العقد إلى السكني، بخلاف الأرض والثياب، فإنّهما يختلفان باختلاف المزروع واللابس، فلا بد من البيان.
[٢] قوله : وله كل عمل ؛ يعني وللمستأجر أن يعمل في الدار والدكان : أي يعمل كل شيء من العمل ؛ لأنها لا تختلف باختلافِ العامل والعمل، فجاز له أن يعمل فيه ما شاء عند الإطلاق، فله أن يتوضأ ويغتسل ويغسل الثياب، ويكسر الحطب المعتاد، ويستنجي : بحائطه، ويدق فيه وتداً، ويربط الدواب في موضع معتاد له، ويسكنها من أحبب، سواء كان بإجارة أو غيرها وليس للأجير أن يدخل دابته الدار المستأجرة بعدما سكن المستأجر. وذكر في «النهاية» وغيرها : لا يدخل الدواب في عرفنا ؛ لأنَّ المنازل ببخارا تضيق عن سكنى الناس، فكيف تتسع بإدخال الدواب، وإنما هذا الجواب بناء على عرفهم في الكوفة ، فحاصله : إنَّ العمل مبني على العــرف ، وذكر في «الذخيرة» وغيرها : إنما يكون له ربط الدواب إذا كان فيها موضع معد لذلك ، وأما إذا لم يكن فليس له ذلك.
[۳] قوله : سوى موهن البناء ؛ أي سوى عمل يوهن البناء، ويضعفه كالقصارة والحدادة والطحن، فلا يسكن قصاراً ولا حداداً ولا طحانا ؛ لأنه فيه ضرراً ظاهراً، فإنه يوهن البناء، فيتقيد العقد بما ورائها دلالة ، إلا إذا رضي المالك، أو اشترط ذلك في عقد الإجارة.
ولو انهدم البناء بهذه الأشياء وجب عليه الضمان؛ لأنه متعد فيها، ولا أجر عليه ؛ لأنَّ الضمان والأجر لا يجتمعان ، وإن لم ينهدم وجب عليه الأجر استحساناً ، والقياس أن لا يجب.
ولو اختلفا في اشتراط ذلك كان القولُ للمؤجر ؛ لأنه لو أنكر أصل العقد كان القول له ، فكذا إذا أنكر نوعاً من الانتفاع ولو أقاما البينة كانت بينة المستأجر أولى ؛ لأنها تثبت الزيادة. كما صرحوا به.
Chapter: What is permissible in hire and what is not. It is valid to rent a house or shop without mentioning what work will be done in it, because the customary use in them is residence, so the contract is directed to that. And the lessee has every work except that which weakens the building, like fulling. [1] He said: "And it is valid to rent..." etc.; by analogy it should not be valid because the purpose of the house and shop is usufruct, and it may be for residence or for storing goods, so it should not be permissible unless the use is specified, like land for cultivation and clothes for wearing. The basis of istihsan is what the commentator mentioned: "because the customary use..." etc. The summary: The customary use in them is residence, and custom is like a condition, so the contract is directed to residence, unlike land and clothes, because they vary by what is grown or worn, so specification is necessary. [2] He said: "And he has every work"; meaning the lessee may do in the house and shop any work, because they do not vary by the worker or the work, so it is permissible for him to do whatever he wishes when unrestricted. Thus he may perform ablution, bathe, wash clothes, break ordinary firewood, relieve himself against its wall, drive a peg, tie animals in a customary place, and let reside whomever he wishes, whether by hire or otherwise. The hireling may not bring his animal into the hired house after the lessee has resided. It is mentioned in al-Nihayah and others: He may not bring animals in our custom because the houses in Bukhara are too narrow for people's residence, so how can they accommodate animals? This answer is based on their custom in Kufa. The summary: This is based on custom. It is mentioned in al-Dhakhirah and others: He may tie animals only if there is a prepared place for that; if not, he may not. [3] He said: "Except weakening the building"; i.e., except work that weakens and debilitates the building, like fulling, blacksmithing, and milling. So he may not house a fuller, blacksmith, or miller because it causes manifest harm. It weakens the building, so the contract is implicitly restricted to other than these, unless the owner consents or it is stipulated in the hire contract. If the building collapses due to these things, he must guarantee because he transgressed in them, and he is not owed rent because guarantee and rent do not combine. If it does not collapse, rent is owed istihsanan, but by analogy it is not owed. If they dispute over stipulating that, the word is the lessor's, because if he denied the original contract his word is taken, so also if he denies a type of usufruct. If they both produce evidence, the lessee's evidence is preferred because it establishes an increase, as they have explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on hire (ijarah) concerning the validity of renting a house or shop without specifying the use. The author states that it is valid, and the default use is residence based on custom (urf). The commentator then provides the legal reasoning: by qiyas (analogy) with land or clothes, specification would be required, but istihsan (juristic preference) overrides qiyas because custom is akin to a stipulated condition. The lessee is entitled to any customary use that does not weaken the structure, such as washing, breaking wood, or tying animals, subject to custom. Works that weaken the building, like fulling (qasara), blacksmithing, or milling, are prohibited unless the owner consents. If the lessee performs such weakening work and the building collapses, he must compensate and forfeits rent; if the building does not collapse, rent is still due by istihsan, though qiyas would deny it. In case of dispute over whether such work was stipulated, the lessor's word is taken, but if both have evidence, the lessee's evidence prevails because it proves an additional right. This shows the Hanafi reliance on custom and istihsan to achieve equitable outcomes.

Key Terms
موهن البناء (muwahin al-bina'): Work that weakens or debilitates the building structure, such as fulling, blacksmithing, milling.
القصارة (al-qasara): Fulling, i.e., beating and washing cloth to thicken it; an example of work that weakens the building.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to custom or necessity; used here to validate the lease based on customary use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The lease is valid for customary use, excluding weakening acts.
D) The lease is valid only if the lessor later approves the use.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, custom (urf) can substitute for express stipulation in lease contracts, and istihsan overrides strict analogical reasoning to accommodate common practice.

Renting Land for Building or Planting and End-of-Term Obligations
Ruling on validity and end-of-term obligations for leases of land for building/planting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of leasing land for building or planting, explain the default obligation to remove improvements at the end of the term, and apply the exception where the lessor pays the value of improvements as uprooted.

Core Idea
A lease for building or planting is valid, but the lessee must remove structures unless the owner pays their value as uprooted.

In this passage, we examine a key ruling in Hanafi lease law: whether one can validly rent land for building or planting, and what happens when the lease ends. The author establishes that the lease is valid because the usufruct is known, then sets forth the lessee's obligation to return the land empty—unless the landowner chooses to keep the improvements by paying their value as uprooted. This nuance reflects the Hanafi concern for balancing interests and preventing harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The passage begins by stating that leasing land for building or planting is valid. Identify the reason given: the usufruct is known and customarily contracted. Compare with a cultivation lease.
The full text is: 'ولو استأجر أرضاً لبناء أو غرس صح'.
2
Determine the default end-of-term obligation
Upon expiry, the lessee must 'deliver it empty'—i.e., remove all buildings and plants and level the land. Understand why: these improvements have no fixed duration and leaving them would permanently harm the landowner.
The text: 'فإذا انقضت المدة سلّمها فارغة'.
3
Analyze the exception
The phrase 'إلا أن يغرم المؤجر قيمته مقلوعاً' introduces an exception: if the landowner pays the value of the improvements as if they were uprooted, the lessee may leave them. This benefits both parties.
The value is assessed as 'materials only' (not as installed).
4
Differentiate from the cultivation lease
Contrast with the case where land is rented for crops. If the lease ends before harvest, the crop is left until ripe, and the lessee pays an equivalent rent. The difference: crops have a known end; buildings and plants do not.
This is a qiyas/tafriq reasoning: distinguishing the two scenarios.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Imagine a 5-year lease for a warehouse. At expiry, the lessee must demolish the warehouse. But the landowner may opt to pay the value of the materials (as if demolished) and keep the structure. State the outcome.
This shows the practical application of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجر أرضاً لبناء أو غرس صح، فإذا انقضت المدة سلّمها فارغة، إلا أن يغرم المؤجر قيمته مقلوعاً
[1]
ولو استأجر أرضاً لبناء أو غرس صح ، فإذا انقضت المدة سلّمها فارغة"، إلا أن يغرم المؤجر قيمته مقلوعاً
[]
[١] قوله : صح؛ لأنها منفعة معلومة تعقد بعقد الإجارة عادة، فيصح كما لو استأجر الأرض للزراعة.
[٢] قوله : فإذا انقضت المدة.. الخ ؛ أي فإذا انقضت مدة الإجارة طويلة كانت أو قصيرة لزم المستأجر أن يقلع البناء والغرس، ويسلّم الأرض فارغة عنهما ؛ لأنه ليس لهما نهاية معلومة، حتى يتركا إليها، وفي تركها على الدوام ضرر لصاحب الأرض، سواء كان بأجر أو بغيره، فوجب القلع. بخلاف ما إذا استأجرها للزراعة فانقضت مدة الإجارة والزرع لم يدرك ، حيث يترك الزرع على حاله إلى أن يستحصد بأجر المثل ؛ لأن له نهاية معلومة، فأمكن رعاية الجانبين فيه. [ 1 ] قوله : سلّمها فارغة ؛ وعليه تسوية الأرض ؛ لأنه هو المخرّب لها ، فعليه إصلاحها صرح به الحموي، وغيره. [٤] قوله : إلا أن يغرم ... الخ ؛ استثناء من قوله : سَلَّمَها فارغة : يعني يجب عليه قلع البناء والغرس عند مضي المدة إلا أن يغرم المؤجر : أي صاحب الأرض للمستأجر قيمة ذلك البناء أو الغرس مقلوعاً ؛ لأنَّ في ذلك نظراً لهما.
And if one rents land for building or planting, it is valid. Then when the term expires, he must deliver it empty, unless the lessor pays its value as uprooted. [1] He said: "It is valid" because it is a known usufruct that is customarily contracted by a hire contract, so it is valid just as if he rented the land for cultivation. [2] He said: "Then when the term expires... etc." i.e., when the lease term expires, whether long or short, the lessee is obliged to uproot the building and plants and deliver the land empty of them, because they have no known end until which they may be left, and leaving them permanently harms the landowner, whether for rent or otherwise, so uprooting is obligatory. Unlike if he rented it for cultivation and the lease expires while the crop has not ripened, then the crop is left as is until harvested for an equivalent rent, because it has a known end, so it is possible to observe both sides. [1] He said: "deliver it empty"; and he must level the land because he is the one who damaged it, so he must repair it, as stated by al-Hamawi and others. [4] He said: "Unless he pays... etc."; this is an exception from his statement "deliver it empty": meaning he must uproot the building and plants at the expiry of the term unless the lessor, i.e., the landowner, pays the lessee the value of that building or plants as uprooted, because there is consideration for both.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of leasing land for building or planting and the obligations at the end of the term. The matn states that such a lease is valid because the usufruct (space for construction/growth) is known and customarily contracted. At the end of the term, the lessee must remove (uproot) the structures and plants and return the land empty, because these improvements have no fixed duration and would permanently harm the owner if left. An exception exists: if the lessor (landowner) pays the value of the improvements as uprooted, the lessee may leave them. This is different from crops, which have a known harvest time and are left until ripeness with rent compensation. The lessee is also responsible for leveling the land (filling holes, etc.) since he caused the damage. The exception gives the lessor an option to retain the improvements by paying their value as uprooted, benefiting both parties. This shows the Hanafi concern for avoiding harm (darar) and balancing interests.

Key Terms
مقلوعاً (maqlu'an): In a state of being uprooted or torn down; the building or plants are valued as if removed from the ground.
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The equivalent rent; the market rate for similar usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to structures erected by the lessee on rented land when the lease expires according to the Hanafi text?
A) They become the property of the landowner automatically.
B) They must be removed by the lessee, unless the landowner pays their value as uprooted.
C) They may remain if the lessee continues paying rent.
D) They become jointly owned by lessee and landowner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a lessee must remove improvements at lease end unless the lessor opts to retain them by paying their value as uprooted.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease

10 min
Ruling on Leasing Land for Building or Planting and Termination
Explains ruling of lease termination for building/planting with conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lease termination for building/planting, including the conditions under which the lessor may pay the value and take ownership without the lessee's consent.

Core Idea
Building/planting lease ends with mandatory removal, but lessor may keep improvements by paying value if uprooting harms the land.

When you lease land for building or planting, the lease is valid, but at the end of the term, the land must be returned empty. However, Hanafi fiqh provides an exception to protect both parties: the lessor can pay the value of the improvements and keep them if removing them would damage the land. This lesson unpacks the rule, the exception, and the nuanced conditions for consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The lease of land for building or planting is valid (sahih) because it is a known benefit. At the end of the term, the lessee must remove all structures and plants and hand over the land empty.
This is the general rule, analogous to leasing land for cultivation.
2
Recognize the exception: lessor pays value
If the lessor pays the lessee the value of the improvements as if they were uprooted (qal'), the lessor may keep them. The phrase 'as uprooted' means the value of the building/plants in their removed state, not their installed value.
This exception prevents harm to the lessee from losing improvements without compensation.
3
Distinguish between harmful and harmless removal
If uprooting damages the land (e.g., leaves holes), the lessor may unilaterally pay the value and take ownership without the lessee's consent. If no harm, the lessor needs the lessee's consent.
The key factor is whether removal causes harm to the land.
4
Understand the lessor's option to leave improvements
The lessor may also consent to leave the improvements in place, either for free (as a loan of the land) or for rent (new lease). Al-Burjandi adds that the lessee's consent is also required for this option.
This is a separate option from paying value.
5
Apply the rules on liability and disputes
If the building collapses due to the lessee's transgression, he must compensate but pays no rent. If it stands, rent is due by istihsan. In disputes about stipulations, the lessor's word is accepted unless the lessee provides stronger evidence.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case to protect the lessor's benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجر أرضاً لبناء أو غرس صح، فإذا انقضت المدة سلمها فارغة، إلا أن يغرم المؤجر قيمته مقلوعاً يوهن البناء، فيتقيد العقد بما ورائها دلالة ، إلا إذا رضي المالك، أو اشترط ذلك في عقد الإجارة.
ولو انهدم البناء بهذه الأشياء وجب عليه الضمان؛ لأنه متعد فيها، ولا أجر عليه ؛ لأنَّ الضمان والأجر لا يجتمعان ، وإن لم ينهدم وجب عليه الأجر استحساناً ، والقياس أن لا يجب.
ولو اختلفا في اشتراط ذلك كان القولُ للمؤجر ؛ لأنه لو أنكر أصل العقد كان القول له ، فكذا إذا أنكر نوعاً من الانتفاع ولو أقاما البينة كانت بينة المستأجر أولى ؛ لأنها تثبت الزيادة. كما صرحوا به.
[١] قوله : صح؛ لأنها منفعة معلومة تعقد بعقد الإجارة عادة، فيصح كما لو استأجر الأرض للزراعة.
[٢] قوله : فإذا انقضت المدة.. الخ ؛ أي فإذا انقضت مدة الإجارة طويلة كانت أو قصيرة لزم المستأجر أن يقلع البناء والغرس، ويسلّم الأرض فارغة عنهما ؛ لأنه ليس لهما نهاية معلومة، حتى يتركا إليها، وفي تركها على الدوام ضرر لصاحب الأرض، سواء كان بأجر أو بغيره، فوجب القلع. بخلاف ما إذا استأجرها للزراعة فانقضت مدة الإجارة والزرع لم يدرك ، حيث يترك الزرع على حاله إلى أن يستحصد بأجر المثل ؛ لأن له نهاية معلومة، فأمكن رعاية الجانبين فيه. [ 1 ] قوله : سلّمها فارغة ؛ وعليه تسوية الأرض ؛ لأنه هو المخرّب لها ، فعليه إصلاحها صرح به الحموي، وغيره. [٤] قوله : إلا أن يغرم ... الخ ؛ استثناء من قوله : سَلَّمَها فارغة : يعني يجب عليه قلع البناء والغرس عند مضي المدة إلا أن يغرم المؤجر : أي صاحب الأرض للمستأجر قيمة ذلك البناء أو الغرس مقلوعاً ؛ لأنَّ في ذلك نظراً لهما.
ويتملكه بلا رضا المستأجر إن نقص القلعُ الأرض، وإلا فبرضاه، أو يرضى بتركه، فيكون البناء والغرس لهذا، والأرض لهذا
وهذا إذا كانت الأرض لا تنقص بالقلع ؛ لأنَّ الواجب دفع الضرر عنها ، فإذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع تضرراً به فكان له دفع هذا الضرر بدفع القيمة إلى المستأجر، وينفرد به ؛ لأن المستأجر لا يتضرر بذلك، إذ الكلام في مستحق القلع ، والقيمة تقوم مقامه.
وإن كانت الأرض لا تنقص بالقلع وأراد أن يضمن له قيمته ويكون له البناء ، فليس له ذلك إلا برضـا صاحبه ؛ لاستوائهما في ثبوت الملك، وعدم ترجح أحدهما على الآخر، فلا بد من اتفاقهما في الترك، بخلاف القلع، حيث ينفرد به أحدهما في هذه الحالة دون الآخر. (٢) قوله : ويتملكه بلا رضى المستأجر ؛ أي سواء رضي المستأجر بذلك الغرم والتملك أو لا ؛ لأنَّ في قلعه ضرراً للمؤجر ، وفي إبقائه وإعطاء القيمة لا ضرر للمستأجر. [٢] قوله : وإلا فيرضاه ؛ أي وإن لم تنقص الأرض بالقلع فإنّما يغرم المؤجر القيمة ويتملكه إن رضي به المستأجرُ فلا يتملكه إن لم يرض به ؛ لأنَّ الشجر والبناء للمستأجر، وفي قلعه لا ضرر.
(۳) قوله : أو يرضى...الخ ؛ أي أو يرضى المؤجر بترك البناء أو الغرس على حاله ؛ لأنَّ الحق لرب الأرض ؛ فإذا رضي باستمرار البناء أو الغرس بإجراء بغير أجر كان له ذلك، وهذا الترك من المؤجر يكون عارية لأرضه إن كان بغير أجر، وإجارة إن كان بأجر.
وقال البرْجَندِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية»: والظاهرُ أنّ رضاء المستأجر شرط في ذلك أيضاً، حتى لو رضي المؤجرُ بالترك، ولم يرض المستأجر به ، فله أن يقلع ذلك. انتهى.
فلو رجع الضمير في قوله : يرضى إلى كل من المؤجر والمستأجر لكان أولى.
And if one leases land for building or planting, it is valid. Then when the term ends, he hands it over empty, unless the lessor pays its value as uprooted. [The phrase] 'weakens the building' is not in the original; it is a comment. The contract is restricted by indication to what is beyond it, unless the owner consents or that is stipulated in the lease contract. And if the building collapses due to these things, he must compensate because he is transgressing, and he is not due rent because compensation and rent do not combine. If it does not collapse, rent is due by istihsan, but analogy would be that it is not due. And if they dispute about the stipulation of that, the word is of the lessor; because if he denied the original contract, his word would be accepted, so likewise if he denies a type of benefit. And if they produce evidence, the evidence of the lessee is given precedence because it proves an increase, as they have stated. [1] His saying: 'it is valid' because it is a known benefit customarily contracted by a lease contract, so it is valid just as leasing land for cultivation. [2] His saying: 'then when the term ends...' i.e., when the lease term ends, whether long or short, the lessee must uproot the building and planting and hand over the land empty of them because they have no known end to which they can be left, and leaving them perpetually harms the landowner, whether with rent or without, so uprooting is obligatory. Unlike if he leased it for cultivation and the lease term ends while the crop has not ripened, where the crop is left as it is until it can be harvested with the usual rent because it has a known end, so it is possible to consider both sides. [1] His saying: 'hand it over empty'; and he must level the land because he is the one who damaged it, so he must repair it, as stated by al-Hamawi and others. [4] His saying: 'unless the lessor pays...' an exception to his saying: 'hand it over empty': meaning he must uproot the building and planting at the end of the term unless the lessor, i.e., the landowner, pays the lessee the value of that building or planting as uprooted, because that is consideration for both. And he takes ownership of it without the lessee's consent if uprooting harms the land; otherwise, with his consent, or he consents to its being left, so the building and planting belong to this one and the land to that one. And this is if the land is not harmed by uprooting; because the obligation is to repel harm from it. If the land is harmed by uprooting, he is harmed by it, so he can repel this harm by paying the value to the lessee, and he [the lessor] takes sole ownership because the lessee is not harmed by that, since the discussion is about the one deserving uprooting, and the value takes its place. And if the land is not harmed by uprooting and he wants to guarantee its value and have the building, that is not permissible except with the owner's consent, because they are equal in establishing ownership and neither is preponderant, so agreement on leaving is necessary, unlike uprooting where one party alone takes ownership in this case without the other. (2) His saying: 'and he takes ownership of it without the lessee's consent' i.e., whether the lessee consents to that payment and ownership or not, because uprooting harms the lessor, and leaving it and giving the value does not harm the lessee. [2] His saying: 'otherwise, with his consent' i.e., if the land is not harmed by uprooting, then the lessor pays the value and takes ownership only if the lessee consents; he does not take ownership if he does not consent, because the trees and building belong to the lessee, and uprooting does not harm. (3) His saying: 'or he consents...' i.e., or the lessor consents to leaving the building or planting as it is, because the right is of the landowner; if he consents to the continuation of the building or planting with or without rent, that is permissible. And this leaving by the lessor is a loan of his land if without rent, and a lease if with rent. And al-Burjandi said in Sharh al-Niqayah: It appears that the consent of the lessee is also a condition in that, so that if the lessor consents to leaving and the lessee does not consent, then he [the lessee] may uproot it. End. So if the pronoun in his saying 'he consents' referred to both the lessor and the lessee, it would be better.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a ruling from the matn about leasing land for building or planting. The author first states the ruling: such a lease is valid because it involves a known benefit customarily contracted. At the end of the lease term, the lessee must remove all structures and plants and hand over the land empty, because these improvements have no fixed end date and their permanent presence harms the owner. The only exception is if the lessor chooses to pay the lessee the value of the improvements as uprooted—i.e., their value if removed—and thereby keep them. The commentary explains that if uprooting damages the land (e.g., by making holes), the lessor may unilaterally pay the value and take ownership without the lessee's consent, because otherwise the land would be harmed. If uprooting does not harm the land, the lessor can only take ownership with the lessee's consent, since the improvements belong to the lessee and removing them causes no harm. Alternatively, the lessor may consent to leave the improvements in place; this constitutes a loan (if free) or a new lease (if for rent). Al-Burjandi adds that the lessee's consent is also required for leaving the improvements. The passage also covers cases where the building collapses due to external causes: if the lessee caused it by transgression (e.g., negligence), he must compensate but pays no rent; if it does not collapse, rent is due by istihsan (equitable preference) despite Qiyas (analogy) suggesting otherwise. In disputes about stipulations, the lessor's word is taken unless the lessee provides stronger evidence. The commentary consistently uses the analytical tools of Hanafi fiqh: distinguishing between harmful and harmless removal, applying istihsan, and prioritizing the lessor's default position.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Uprooting or removing the building/plants from the land at lease termination.
غرم (ghurm): Payment of the value of the improvements as uprooted by the lessor to the lessee in order to keep them.
مؤجر (mu'ajjir): The lessor, owner of the land who leases it.
مستأجر (musta'jir): The lessee, who rents the land for building or planting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the lessor have the right to pay the value of the building and take ownership without the lessee's consent?
A) Whenever the lessor wants to keep the building
B) Only if uprooting the building would harm the land
C) Only if the lessee agrees
D) Never, the lessee must always remove the building

Key Takeaway
A building/planting lease requires removal at term, but the lessor may pay the value and keep improvements, unilaterally only if removal harms the land.
