Obligation to Remove Buildings and Trees from Leased Land
Explanation of lessee's duty to vacate and two exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default obligation of the lessee upon lease expiry and identify the two exceptions where the lessee may leave buildings or trees.

Core Idea
Lessee must remove structures and trees at lease end, unless lessor pays value (if removal damages land) or consents to retention.

When a lease ends, what happens to buildings or trees the lessee erected on the property? The Hanafi school holds that the lessee must remove them (قلع) to restore the land to its original condition. However, two important exceptions protect both parties' interests: if removal would damage the land, the lessor must compensate and take ownership; if no damage, the lessor may choose to let the items remain. This commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies the rule and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default obligation
State what the lessee must do at the end of the lease period according to the Hanafi ruling.
The lessee must hand over the land empty of anything he added.
2
Understand Exception 1: Compulsory payment by lessor
If removing the building or tree would damage the land, the lessor must pay their value (as removed) and then owns them. This is compulsory; the lessee's consent is not required.
The lessee loses the right to remove in this case.
3
Understand Exception 2: Voluntary consent by lessor
If removal does not damage the land, the lessor may agree to leave the items. In this case, the lessee may also choose to remove them.
Both parties must consent; it is not compulsory.
4
Distinguish perennial plants from crops
Perennial plants (الرطبة) that persist in the ground are treated like trees, not like annual crops (الزرع) that have a fixed harvest time.
Crops cannot be forced to be uprooted before harvest.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a situation with multiple items, determine which exception applies to each based on whether removal causes damage.
Evaluate each item separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرطبة كالشجر، فإنَّ لها بقاء في الأرض بخلاف الزرع، فإنّه فإذا انقضت المدَّة لا يجبر على القلع قبل أوان الحصاد. قوله : ويتملكه بالنصب عطفٌ على أن يغرم وإلا : أي وإن لم ينقص القلعُ الأرض. قوله : أو يرضى عطفٌ على يغرم" فالحاصل أنه يجب على المستأجر أن يسلّمها فارغة إلا أن يوجد أحد الأمرين : الأول : أن يعطي المؤجر قيمة البناء أو الغرس مقلوعاً ويتملكه، وهذا الإعطاء والتملك يكون جبراً على تقدير أن ينقص القلعُ الأرض، ويكون برضاء المستأجر على تقدير أن لا ينقص القلعُ الأرض. والأمر الثاني : أن يرضى المؤجّرُ بترك البناء أو الغرس في أرضه. هذا الذي ذكره في وجوب القلع وعدم وجوبه ، وفهم منه ولاية القلع للمستأجر وعدمها ؛ فإنَّه قد ذكر أنه إن نقص القلع الأرض يتملكه بلا رضا المستأجر، فحينئذ لا يكونُ للمستأجر القلع، وفي غير هذه الصورة يكون.
And the moist plant is like a tree, because it has persistence in the ground unlike crops, so when the period ends he is not forced to uproot before harvest time. His statement: 'And he owns it' – accusative, coordinated with 'that he pays' and 'otherwise' meaning: even if uprooting does not damage the land. His statement: 'or he consents' – coordinated with 'he pays'. So the conclusion is that it is obligatory upon the lessee to hand it over empty unless one of two things exists: First, that the lessor gives the value of the building or planting uprooted and owns it, and this giving and owning is compulsory if uprooting damages the land, and is with the consent of the lessee if uprooting does not damage the land. Second, that the lessor consents to leaving the building or planting in his land. This is what he mentioned regarding the obligation and non-obligation of removal, and from it is understood the lessee's authority to remove and its absence. For he mentioned that if uprooting damages the land, he owns it without the lessee's consent, then the lessee has no right to remove, and in other cases he does.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from a commentary (Sharh) on Al-Wiqayah. It clarifies the lessee's obligation to vacate the leased land after the lease expires. The default rule is that the lessee must remove any buildings or trees he planted (قلع). However, there are two exceptions where the lessee may leave them: First, if removal would damage the land, the landlord must pay the value of the items (as removed) and then owns them – this is compulsory on the landlord. Second, if removal does not damage the land, the landlord may voluntarily agree to let the items remain; in this case the lessee may also choose to remove them. The text also distinguishes between crops (زرع) that have a known end (must be harvested, so cannot be forced to uproot before harvest) and perennial plants (رطبة) like alfalfa that have persistent roots – these are treated like trees, so the lessee must remove them unless the landlord exercises one of the two options. The reasoning is based on protecting both parties' interests: the lessee's right to profit and the landlord's right to the land's integrity.

Key Terms
الرطبة (al-ratba): Perennial plants whose roots persist in the ground (e.g., alfalfa), treated like trees in removal rulings.
القلع (al-qal'): Uprooting or removing structures/plants from leased land after lease expiry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if removing a tree from leased land damages the land, what happens?
A) The lessee must still remove it.
B) The landlord must pay the tree's value and then owns it.
C) The land is left as is.
D) The lessee may keep the tree.

Key Takeaway
The lessee must remove his structures and trees unless the landlord pays for them (if removal damages) or consents to them staying (if no damage).

Liability for Adding a Rider on a Hired Animal
Ruling on liability when a second rider causes the animal's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a second rider causes the death of a hired animal and explain the reasoning based on qiyas.

Core Idea
Liability for a second rider is half the animal's value—weight is irrelevant; it's the act of riding that counts.

This passage addresses a common scenario in ijarah: a hired animal perishes after a second person rides it. The Hanafi school uses a qiyas analogy to wounding to determine liability. Understanding this ruling helps clarify how Hanafi law handles uncertain causation in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the principal rider allowing a second rider (رديف) on a hired animal, leading to the animal's death. The ruling: the principal rider pays half the animal's value.
This is a wajib compensation (daman).
2
Locate the dalil (qiyas analogy)
The proof is a qiyas to the case of two people wounding a third: if the cause of death is uncertain, liability is halved. The analogy is that here too the death is attributed equally to both riders' acts.
The illa is that the specific cause of death cannot be determined.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (why the analogy holds)
Just as wounding can cause death regardless of number or severity, the act of riding can cause death regardless of weight. The Hanafi argue weight is not a reliable indicator, so number of riders is considered.
This refutes the idea that a heavier rider should pay more.
4
Note the exceptions
The ruling applies only if the animal can carry both. If not, full value is due. Also, if the second rider is a child or carried on the shoulder, different rules apply (proportional weight or full value). The hire is also due separately.
These exceptions show the scope of the general rule.
5
Understand the coexistence of hire and liability
The text resolves a potential confusion: the principal rider owes both hire (for his own riding) and compensation (for the second rider's benefit). They are for different uses, so no contradiction.
Hire is for the primary rider's benefit; liability is for the additional rider's act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضمِنَ بإرداف رجل معه وقد ذكر ركوبه : أي ركوب المستأجر من غير [١]قوله : عطف على أن يغرم ؛ بل على قوله : يغرم ؛ ليكون منصوباً داخل تحت إن. ثم قال: (نصف قيمتها بلا اعتبار النقل) رجلاً آخر فعطَبت الدابةُ ضَمِنَ نصف قيمتها، سواء كان أخف أو أثقل ؛ لأن تلف الدابة إنما حصل من ركوب ذلك الآخر، ولا ينشأ من الثقل، إذ رب ثقيل يحسنُ الركوب فلا يضر ثقله بالدابة، وخفيف لا يحسنه فيضر بها ؛ ولأنَّ الآدمي لا يوزنُ عادة، فاعتبر فيه العدد. وصار كما لو جرح إنسان إنساناً جراحة ، وجرحه آخر جراحات، ينتصف الضمان إذ ربّما هلك الإنسان من جراحةٍ واحدة، وسلم من عدد كثير منها، وهذا إذا كانت الدابة تطيق حمل الاثنين، وإن لم تطق ذلك فيضمن كل القيمة. صرّح به في «الكافي»، وغيره. هذا وقيد بالأرداف ؛ لأنه لو ركبها وحمل على عاتقه غيره يضمن كل القيمة ، وإن كانت الدابة تطيق ذلك ؛ لأنَّ ثقل الراكب مع الذي حمله يجتمعان في مكان واحد، فيكون أشق عليها ، كما لو حملها مثل وزن الحنطة حديداً. وقيد بالرجل ؛ لأن الرديف إذا كان صبياً لا يستمسك، يضمن بقدر ثقله. اعلم أن المصنف ذكر ضمان نصف القيمة، ولم يذكر كم يجب عليه من الأجر، وفي «المحيط»: يجب عليه جميع الأجرة إذا هلكت الدابة بعدما بلغت مقصده، ونصفها إذا كان قبله، ثم للمالك الخيارُ إن شاءَ ضَمِّنَ الرَّديف وإن شاء ضَمَّنَ الرَّاكب، فالراكب لا يرجع بما ضَمِن ، والرَّديف يرجعُ إن كان مستأجراً وإلا فلا ، كما صرحوا فإن اختلج في صدرك أن الأجر والضمان لا يجتمعان، فكيف اجتمعا في هذه المسألة، فأزحه بأنَّ الأجر في مقابلة ما استوفاه من منفعة ركوبه ولا ضمان فيه ، والضمان في مقابلة ما استوفاه رديفه من منفعة ركوبه ، ولا أجر فاختلف المحـلان والسببان، فلا تنافي. [ ١ ] قوله : الثقل ؛ - بكسر الثاء، وفتح القاف : خلافُ الخفّة، – وبكسر الثاء - وسكون القاف - : الحمل - وبفتحتين - : متاع المسافر. ذكره الأَثْقَانِي.
And he is liable for adding another man with him while his riding has been mentioned: meaning the riding of the lessee without [1] his statement: coordinated with 'that he pays'; rather on his statement 'he pays' to be accusative under 'in'. Then he said: (Half its value without considering weight) another man, and the animal perished; he is liable for half its value, whether he was lighter or heavier. Because the destruction of the animal arose only from the riding of that other, and does not originate from weight, since sometimes a heavy person rides well and his weight does not harm the animal, and a light person who does not ride well harms it. And because humans are not usually weighed, so number is considered. And it becomes like when one person wounds another with one wound, and another wounds him with several wounds; the liability is halved, because sometimes a person dies from one wound and survives many. And this is if the animal can bear the load of both, otherwise he is liable for the full value. It is stated in al-Kafi and others. This is restricted to 'with another rider' because if he rode and carried another on his shoulder, he is liable for the full value, even if the animal can bear it, because the weight of the rider and the one carried gather in one place, making it harder, as if he loaded it with iron of equal weight of wheat. And restricted to 'man' because if the additional rider is a child who cannot hold himself, he is liable according to his weight. Know that the author mentioned liability for half the value, and did not mention how much hire is due. In al-Muhit: All the hire is due if the animal perished after reaching its destination, and half if before. Then the owner has the option: if he wishes, he can claim from the additional rider, and if he wishes, from the principal rider. The principal rider cannot reclaim what he paid, but the additional rider can reclaim if he was the hirer, otherwise not, as they explicitly stated. If it occurs to you that hire and liability cannot coexist, how are they combined in this issue? Remove this doubt by saying: hire is in exchange for the benefit of his own riding, and there is no liability in it; liability is in exchange for the benefit of the additional rider's riding, and there is no hire. So the loci and causes differ, thus no contradiction. [1] His statement: al-thiql – with kasra of tha and fatha of qaf: opposite of lightness; with kasra of tha and sukun of qaf: load; with two fatha: traveller's baggage. Mentioned by al-Athqani.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the same commentary, addressing a specific scenario in ijarah: when a person hires a riding animal and allows another person (رديف) to ride behind him. The ruling: if the animal dies because of this, the principal rider must pay half the animal's value. The reasoning is that the death is attributed to the second rider's act of riding, regardless of weight – because weight is not reliably correlated with harm, and humans are not typically weighed. This is analogized to a case where two people wound a person: liability is halved because the cause of death is uncertain. If the animal could not carry both, the principal rider pays full value. Additional distinctions: if the second person is carried on the shoulders (حمله على عاتقه), full value is paid because the weight concentrates. If the second rider is a child, liability is proportional to weight. The text also discusses hire: the principal rider owes the full hire if the animal reached its destination, half otherwise. The owner may choose to claim compensation from either rider, with rules for recourse. A potential confusion (that hire and compensation cannot coexist) is resolved by noting they are for different benefits (the rider's own riding vs. the additional rider's).

Key Terms
الرديف (al-radif): The person who rides behind the principal rider on the same animal.
الأجر (al-ajr): Hire paid for the benefit of riding; here it is due from the principal rider even if the animal dies, based on destination reached.
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation for damage or loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The full value of the camel.
B) Half the value of the camel.
C) Nothing.
D) The full hire only.

Key Takeaway
The principal rider is liable for half the animal's value when a second rider causes its death, because the cause of death is uncertain and attributed equally.

Liability of the Pillion Rider (Radif) in Hire
Ruling on radif's liability with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of a pillion rider (radif) when a hired animal dies, and apply the Hanafi qiyas that splits liability.

Core Idea
A pillion rider is liable for half the animal's value, because causation is split regardless of weight—like two wounders.

When you hire a riding animal and let someone ride behind you, you may wonder who compensates the owner if the animal dies. The Hanafi school provides a clear rule: the pillion rider (radif) is liable for half the animal's value, irrespective of weight. This ruling is derived by qiyas to the case of two wounders where liability is split equally. Let's examine the reasoning and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a hired animal dying while carrying two riders. The ruling: the pillion rider (radif) must pay half the animal's value to the owner.
This applies when the animal could bear both riders.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the case of two people wounding a victim: if one gives a single wound and the other gives multiple wounds, each is liable for half the blood-money.
The source text provides this analogy explicitly.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof works)
The effective cause (illah) is that death can be caused by a single action regardless of number or weight. A single wound may kill, and one rider's action may cause the animal's death. Therefore, liability is shared equally.
The text refutes the idea that weight alone matters, citing skill and the fact that humans are not weighed.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (if any)
Some scholars might argue that weight should determine liability, but the Hanafi position is that the number of riders is decisive.
This is a mukhtalaf fih; the mufta bihi is the half-share.
5
Evaluate the practical distinctions
The ruling changes if the animal cannot bear both (full liability), if the person is carried on the shoulders (full liability), or if the radif is a child (liability proportional to weight).
These exceptions are given in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة باب ما يجوز من الإجارة وما لا يجوز
نصف قيمتها بلا اعتبار النقل
ذكر الرديف ، (نصف قيمتها بلا اعتبار النقل)

Book of Hire: Chapter on what is permissible and what is not permissible in hire. Half of its value without consideration of weight. Mention of the pillion rider: (Half of its value without consideration of weight) … another man, and the animal died, he is liable for half its value, whether he is lighter or heavier; because the death of the animal resulted only from the riding of that other, and it does not arise from weight, for sometimes a heavy person rides well so his weight does not harm the animal, and a light person who does not ride well harms it; and because a person is not usually weighed, so number is considered. And it becomes like if one person wounds another with one wound, and another wounds him with many wounds, the liability is divided equally, for sometimes a person dies from a single wound and survives many wounds. This is if the animal can bear the weight of both; but if it cannot bear that, then he is liable for the entire value. This is stated in al-Kafi and others. This is restricted to the pillion rider; because if he rode and carried another on his shoulder, he is liable for the entire value, even if the animal can bear that, because the weight of the rider and the one he carries come together in one place, making it harder for the animal, like if one carried on it iron equal to the weight of wheat. And it is restricted to a man; because if the pillion rider is a child who cannot hold on, he is liable according to his weight.
Know that the author mentioned liability for half the value, but did not mention how much hire (ujr) is due. In al-Muheet: All the hire is due if the animal dies after reaching its destination, and half if before. Then the owner has the option: if he wishes, he can hold the pillion rider liable, and if he wishes, he can hold the rider liable. The rider cannot recover what he paid, but the pillion rider can recover if he was the hirer, otherwise not, as they have stated.
If it occurs to your heart that hire and liability cannot coexist, and how they coexist in this issue, then remove it by saying: The hire is in exchange for the benefit he (the rider) derived from his riding, and there is no liability in it; and the liability is in exchange for what his pillion rider derived from the benefit of his riding, and there is no hire (from him). So the two subject matters and the two causes differ, hence no contradiction.
[1] His saying: al-thiqal – with kasr of tha and fatha of qaf: the opposite of lightness, and with kasr of tha and sukun of qaf: load, and with two fathas: the traveller's luggage. Mentioned by al-Athqani.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It discusses the liability of the pillion rider (radif) when a hired animal dies. The author states that if a person hires an animal and takes another person (radif) as a pillion rider, and the animal dies, the radif is liable for half the value of the animal, whether he is lighter or heavier than the first rider. The reasoning is that the death occurs from the riding of the radif, not merely from weight, because a heavy person who rides skillfully may cause less harm than a light person who is unskilled. Additionally, humans are not typically weighed, so the number of riders is considered. The author draws an analogy to two people wounding a person: if one wounds once and the other wounds multiple times, the liability is split equally (half each) because death could result from a single wound. This applies if the animal can bear both riders; if not, the radif is liable for the full value. The passage also distinguishes the case where the rider carries someone on his shoulders (then he is liable for full value). And if the radif is a child who cannot hold on, liability is proportional to his weight. Then it discusses the combination of hire (ujr) and compensation (daman), explaining that the owner can claim either from the rider or the radif, and the radif may have recourse if he was the hirer. A potential objection that ujr and daman cannot coexist is resolved by noting they arise from different sources.

Key Terms
الرديف (al-radīf): The person who rides behind the main rider on the same animal.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for the value of the animal in case of damage or loss due to fault.
الأجر (al-ajr): The hire fee paid for the use of the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Half the value of the animal
C) Nothing, because he had permission
D) Half the hire fee

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a pillion rider's liability for a hired animal's death is half its value, irrespective of weight, based on qiyas to wounding liability.

Liability for Overloading or Mistreating a Hired Animal
Rulings on liability when the hirer overloads or beats the animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for overloading a hired animal and distinguish between cases where the animal can or cannot bear the extra load.

Core Idea
Overloading liability hinges on the animal's capacity: excess weight only if bearable, full value if not.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on a hirer's liability when he overloads or mistreats a hired animal. The ruling balances permission and causation, leading to nuanced liability depending on the animal's capacity and who loaded it. We will work through the core ruling and the intra-Hanafi differences on joint loading scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What exactly does the ruling cover? Overloading (ziyada 'ala al-haml) and mistreatment (darb or kabh al-lijam). The ruling states that liability attaches depending on whether the animal can bear the excess load.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios of overloading
If the animal can bear the total load, the hirer is liable only for the excess weight. If the animal cannot bear it, the hirer is liable for the full value. This is because the cause of death is divided between the authorized load and the unauthorized excess.
The principle: 'reason is divided' (al-sabab yanqasim).
3
Note the effect of the owner's participation in loading
If only the owner loads the animal, the hirer is not liable. If both owner and hirer load together, liability is shared. The relied-upon position (from al-Muheet) is half, but al-Khulasa says a quarter.
The difference arises from how the 'permission' for the owner's loading is viewed.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference
The text presents two positions: half (from al-Muheet) and quarter (from al-Khulasa). The author does not explicitly prefer one, but the discussion leans toward half in the case of joint loading.
This is a legitimate scholarly difference within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالزيادة على حِمْلٍ ذكرَ ما زادَ النّقلُ إن أطاق حمله، وإلا كل قيمتها، كعطبها بضربه وكبحه اللجام
[٢]-

، وغيره.
[٢) قوله : أي ضمن ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا استأجر الدابة ليحمل عليها مقداراً، فحمل عليها أكثر منه فعطبت ، فإن كانت الدابةُ تطيق مثل ذلك الحمل ضمن ما زادَ الثقل ؛ لأنها هلكت بمأذون فيه، وغير مأذون ، والسبب الثقل، فانقسم عليهما، وهذا إذا حملها المستأجر ، فإن حملها صاحبها بيده وحده، فلا ضمان على المستأجر ؛ لأنَّ صاحبها هو المباشر. صرَّحَ به في «الفصول العمادية». وإن حمل صاحب الدابة الحمل عليها مع المستأجر وجب النصف على المستأجر ؛ لأنها هلكت بفعل يوجب الضمان، وهو فعل المستأجر، وفعل لا يوجبه، وهو فعل صاحب الدابة، فيجب النصفُ على المستأجر ، ويهدر فعل صاحبها. صرح به
في «المحيط».
وذكر في «الخلاصة»: إنّه يضمن ربع القيمة ؛ لأنَّ النصف مأذون فيه، والنصفُ الآخر بغير إذن، وبحملها يضمن نصف هذا النصف نقله صاحب «المنح»، وغيره، وإن
كانت الدابة لم تطق مثل ذلك الحمل فيضمن جميع قيمتها لعدم الإذن فيه. [٣] قوله : كعطبها...الخ ؛ أي كما يضمن جميع القيمة إذا عطبت الدابةُ بضرب المستأجر إياها أو كبحه اللجام. [٤]قوله : وكبح اللجام...الخ ؛ يقال : كبحت الدابة باللجام كبحاً من باب نفع :
(۱) في «الرمز» (۷: ۹۱۲ – ۹۱۳).
And with the increase over a load mentioned, he is liable for what increased (in weight) if the animal can bear that load, otherwise all its value, like its death by his striking or pulling its bridle.
[2] — For a light person ignorant of horsemanship may be more harmful than a heavy person skilled in it. (And with the increase over a load mentioned, he is liable for what increased in weight if the animal can bear that load, otherwise all its value): That is, he is liable for the increase over the mentioned load if the load is such that this animal can bear it, and if the load is not like that he is liable for all its value, (like its death by his striking or pulling its bridle). [1] His saying: horsemanship (al-furosyya); he intended by horsemanship knowledge of how to ride, how to sit on the back of the animal, how to press his thighs, how to put down his legs, how to hold the bridle and halter. Mentioned by al-‘Ayni and others.
[2] His saying: ‘that is, he is liable…’ means: If he hires the animal to carry a certain amount on it, then he carries on it more than that and it dies, then if the animal can bear such a load, he is liable for the increase in weight; because it died by something permitted (the agreed load) and something not permitted (the excess), and the cause is weight, so it is divided between them. This is if the hirer loads it himself. If the owner loads it with his own hand alone, then there is no liability on the hirer, because the owner is the direct cause. This is stated in al-Fusul al-‘Imadiyya. If the owner loads the animal together with the hirer, then half is obligatory on the hirer; because it died by an act that incurs liability (the act of the hirer) and an act that does not incur liability (the act of the owner), so half is obligatory on the hirer and the owner’s act is waived. This is stated in al-Muheet. And it is mentioned in al-Khulasa that he is liable for a quarter of the value; because half is permitted and the other half is without permission, and by loading it he is liable for half of that half. This is transmitted by the author of al-Manh and others. And if the animal cannot bear such a load, then he is liable for all its value due to the lack of permission for it. [3] His saying: ‘like its death…’ i.e., just as he is liable for all the value if the animal dies by the hirer’s striking it or pulling its bridle. [4] His saying: ‘kabh al-lijam…’ It is said: kababtu al-dabbata bi al-lijam kaban from the door of nafa‘a: (reference).
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of liability in hire contracts, focusing on overloading and mistreatment. The author explains that if a hirer loads the animal with more than the agreed amount, his liability depends on whether the animal can bear the extra load. If the animal can bear it, he is liable only for the extra weight (the increase); if not, he is liable for the full value. The same applies if he strikes the animal or pulls its bridle (kabh al-lijam), causing its death — he is liable for the full value. The commentary further clarifies scenarios where the owner also participates in loading: if the owner loads alone, the hirer is not liable; if both load, the hirer is liable for half. A transmission from al-Khulasa states the hirer is liable for a quarter in some cases, but the relied-upon position (from al-Muheet) is half. The reasoning is based on distinguishing between authorized and unauthorized acts.

Key Terms
الزيادة على الحمل (al-ziyāda ‘alā al-ḥiml): Carrying more than the agreed load on a hired animal.
الكبح (al-kabḥ): Pulling back the bridle to rein in the animal, which can cause injury or death if done harshly.
الفروسية (al-furūsiyya): Skill in riding, including knowledge of how to sit, hold the reins, and control the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when both the hirer and the owner load the animal together and the animal dies, which view from al-Muheet states the hirer's liability?
A) Full value
B) Half the value
C) Quarter the value
D) No liability

Key Takeaway
A hirer's liability for overloading a hired animal depends on the animal's capacity: if it can bear the total load, he pays only for the excess; otherwise full value, and mistreatment (beating or pulling bridle) always incurs full liability.

Liability for Overloading and Unusual Beating of Hired Animals: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on liability for overloading and beating causing animal to perish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for liability when a hired animal perishes due to overloading or beating, and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding customary beating.

Core Idea
Customary actions in a hire contract are like stipulated conditions; unusual actions trigger full liability.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses when a hirer is liable if a hired animal perishes from overloading or beating. While overloading liability is straightforward—based on permission and capacity—the beating scenario reveals a sharp intra-Hanafi dispute about the role of custom in implied permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Overloading vs beating; note the general rule for overloading: liability for excess if animal can bear the total load, full value if it cannot.
The passage first covers overloading, then compares it to beating.
2
Analyze the overloading details
Examine the division of liability when both owner and hirer load; the effect of direct action by the owner.
Note the different rulings from 'al-Fusul', 'al-Muhit', and 'al-Khulasa'.
3
Pinpoint the intra-Hanafi dispute on beating
Abu Hanifa holds full liability even for customary beating; Sahibayn hold no liability if the beating is customary and moderate.
The key proof: custom implies permission (Sahibayn) versus permission restricted to safety (Abu Hanifa).
4
Evaluate the tarjih and mufta bihi
The commentary notes Imam's retraction to Sahibayn's view, though al-Shami restricts the retraction to a different case.
The mufta bihi is the view of Sahibayn, but later scholars debate the retraction's scope.

[٢]- فإن الخفيف الجاهل بالفروسية قد يكون أضر من الثقيل العالم بها. (وبالزيادةِ على حِمْلٍ ذكرَ ما زادَ النقلُ إن أطاق حمله، وإلا كل قيمتها ) : أي ضمن بالزيادة على حمل ذكر ما زاد إن كان الحمل بحيث تطيقه هذه الدابة ، وإن لم يكن الحمل كذلك يضمن كل قيمتها ، (كعطبها بضربه وكبحه اللجام) [١] قوله : بالفروسية ; أرادَ بالفروسية معرفة كيفية الركوب، كيف يقعد على ظهر الدابة، وكيف يضم فخذيه ، وكيف يحط رجليه، وكيف يمسك اللجام والخطام، ذكره العيني وغيره.
[٢] قوله : أي ضمن ...الخ ; يعني إذا استأجر الدابة ليحمل عليها مقداراً، فحمل عليها أكثر منه فعطبت ، فإن كانت الدابةُ تطيق مثل ذلك الحمل ضمن ما زادَ الثقل ; لأنها هلكت بمأذون فيه، وغير مأذون ، والسبب الثقل، فانقسم عليهما، وهذا إذا حملها المستأجر ، فإن حملها صاحبها بيده وحده، فلا ضمان على المستأجر ; لأنَّ صاحبها هو المباشر. صرَّحَ به في «الفصول العمادية». وإن حمل صاحب الدابة الحمل عليها مع المستأجر وجب النصف على المستأجر ; لأنها هلكت بفعل يوجب الضمان، وهو فعل المستأجر، وفعل لا يوجبه، وهو فعل صاحب الدابة، فيجب النصفُ على المستأجر ، ويهدر فعل صاحبها. صرح به في «المحيط». وذكر في «الخلاصة»: إنّه يضمن ربع القيمة ; لأنَّ النصف مأذون فيه، والنصفُ الآخر بغير إذن، وبحملها يضمن نصف هذا النصف نقله صاحب «المنح»، وغيره، وإن كانت الدابة لم تطق مثل ذلك الحمل فيضمن جميع قيمتها لعدم الإذن فيه. [٣] قوله : كعطبها...الخ ; أي كما يضمن جميع القيمة إذا عطبت الدابةُ بضرب المستأجر إياها أو كبحه اللجام. [٤]قوله : وكبح اللجام...الخ ; يقال : كبحت الدابة باللجام كبحاً من باب نفع :
العطب : الهلاك ، وكبحُ اللّجام: جذبُهُ إلى نفسه عنفاً ، بسبب الضرب أو كبح اللجامِ كل قيمتها عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما لا، إلا أن يكون ضرباً أو كبحاً غير متعارف.
جذبته به لتقف، وأكبحته بالألف والجيم جذبتُ عنانه لينتصب رأسه. كذا في «المصباح». قوله : وعندهما لا...الخ ; يعني وعندهما لا يضمن إذا فعل فعلاً معتاداً متعارفاً، أما في غير المتعارف فيجب الضمان بالإجماع ; لأن الكبح المتعارف أو الضرب المتعارف مما يدخل تحت مطلق العقد ; لكون المعروف عرفاً بمنزلة المشروط شرطاً، وربما لا تكون الدابة منقادة إلا بالضرب أو الكبح فثبت الإذن بالعرف فلا يضمنه. وللإمام: إن الإذن مقيد بشرط السلامة إذ السوق يتحقق بدون الضرب، والكبحُ وهما للبالغة في السير، فيتقيَّدُ الإذن بوصف السلامة، وذكر في «غاية البيان» نقلاً عن «التتمة»: إن الأصح رجوع الإمام إلى قولهما.
وقال العلامة الشامي : ظاهرُهُ إنّ رجوعه في مسألة الصغير دون الدابة ، وينبغي أن يكون كذلك ; لأنَّ مسألةَ الدَّابة جرى عليها أصحاب المتون، فلو ثبت رجوع الإمام فيها لما مشوا على خلافه ; لأنَّ ما رجع عنه المجتهد لم يكن مذهباً له. انتهى.
And with the increase beyond the mentioned load, he is liable for what he increased by transporting if the animal can bear that load, otherwise for its full value, like its perishing due to his beating or harsh reining of the bit. [2]- Indeed, a light (rider) ignorant of horsemanship may be more harmful than a heavy one who is knowledgeable. (And with the increase beyond a mentioned load, he is liable for what he increased by transporting if the animal can bear that load, otherwise for its full value): That is, he is liable due to the increase beyond the load mentioned for what he increased, if the load is such that this animal can bear it, and if the load is not like that, he is liable for its full value. (like its perishing due to his beating or harsh reining of the bit). [1] His saying: 'of horsemanship'; he intended by horsemanship knowledge of the manner of riding, how to sit on the back of the animal, how to draw his thighs, how to put down his legs, how to hold the bit and halter. Mentioned by al-Ayni and others. [2] His saying: 'that is, he is liable... etc.': meaning if a person hires an animal to carry a certain amount, then loads more than that and it perishes, then if the animal can bear such a load, he is liable for the weight of the increase; because it perished by something permitted and something not permitted, and the cause is the weight, so it is divided between them. This is if the hirer loaded it. If the owner of the animal loaded it by himself alone, there is no liability on the hirer, because the owner is the direct doer. It is stated explicitly in 'al-Fusul al-Umadiyya'. If the owner of the animal loaded it together with the hirer, half is due on the hirer; because it perished due to an act that necessitates liability, which is the act of the hirer, and an act that does not necessitate it, which is the act of the owner of the animal, so half is due on the hirer, and the act of the owner is disregarded. It is stated in 'al-Muhit'. And it is mentioned in 'al-Khulasa': he is liable for a quarter of the value; because half is permitted, and the other half is without permission, and by loading it he is liable for half of this half, as quoted by the author of 'al-Minah' and others. And if the animal cannot bear such a load, then he is liable for its entire value due to the lack of permission for it. [3] His saying: 'like its perishing...etc.': meaning just as he is liable for the full value if the animal perishes due to the hirer beating it or harsh reining of the bit. [4] His saying: 'and harsh reining of the bit...etc.': it is said: I reined the animal with the bit, kababtan, from the door of nafa'a: al-atab: perishing, and kabh al-lijam: pulling it towards himself violently. Due to beating or harsh reining, the full value according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two companions, no, unless the beating or reining is unusual. I pulled it with it to stop, and with alif and jim I pulled its reins to raise its head. So in 'al-Misbah'. His saying: 'and according to the two companions, no...etc.': meaning according to the two companions, he is not liable if he does a customary, normal act. As for the unusual one, liability is obligatory by consensus; because customary reining or beating is included under the absolute contract, because what is known customarily is like a stipulated condition. And perhaps the animal is not submissive except by beating or reining, so permission is established by custom, so he is not liable. And for the Imam: permission is restricted by the condition of safety, since driving can occur without beating and reining, and both are for the animal to proceed, so permission is restricted to the description of safety. It is mentioned in 'Ghayat al-Bayan' quoting from 'al-Tatimma': the correct view is that the Imam returned to their position. And Allama al-Shami said: its apparent meaning is that his return is in the issue of the child, not the animal, and it should be so; because in the issue of the animal, the authors of the texts have proceeded upon it, and if the Imam's return were established regarding it, they would not have proceeded contrary to it; because what the mujtahid retracts from is not his madhhab. End.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Wiqayah concerning the liability of a hirer when the hired animal perishes due to overloading or unusual beatings. The commentator first explains the matn: if the hirer loads more than agreed, liability depends on whether the animal could bear the total weight. If it could, he pays only for the extra weight; if not, he pays full value. The text gives an example of perishing by beating or reining, implying full liability. Then the commentator unpacks the overloading scenario in detail, citing various Hanafi works: if the owner alone loads, no liability on hirer; if both load, half liability; a minority view says quarter. For the beating part, an intra-Hanafi dispute emerges: Abu Hanifa holds the hirer liable even for customary beating, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no liability if it is customary, because custom implies permission. The commentary notes that Imam retracted to their view, but al-Shami restricts the retraction to a different case. This shows the importance of custom in determining implied permission in hire contracts.

Key Terms
العطب (al-atab): Perishing, destruction of the animal.
الكبح (al-kabh): Harsh reining, pulling the bit violently.
اللجام (al-lijam): Bridle, bit used to control the horse.
الفروسية (al-furusiyya): Horsemanship, knowledge of proper riding technique.
المتعارف (al-muta'araf): Customary, normal (in context of beating).
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission, either explicit or implied by contract or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Customary actions are like stipulated conditions; unusual actions trigger full liability.

Liability for Crossing Beyond Destination and Returning
Commentary on liability when hirer exceeds destination and returns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on liability when a hirer returns to the agreed destination after deviation and explain why Zufar's analogy to a depositary is rejected.

Core Idea
Deviation turns the hirer into a usurper; returning to the route does not restore permission—only return to the owner discharges liability.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in ijarah: a hirer takes the animal beyond the agreed destination, then returns it to that destination, after which the animal perishes. The key question is whether the return to the destination absolves the hirer of liability. The Hanafi school holds that liability remains, while Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl argues otherwise. Understanding this ruling clarifies the strict nature of possession once a contract is breached.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the point of dispute
A man hires a beast to Qadisiyya, takes it to Kufa, then returns to Qadisiyya, where it perishes. The dispute is whether he is liable for its value.
2
Identify the Hanafi majority position and its basis
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold him liable because he became a usurper (ghasib). Returning to the destination does not return possession to the owner.
3
Identify Zufar's opposing position and his analogy
Zufar says no liability, analogizing to a depositary who returns a deposit and is exonerated.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar's analogy
The depositary returns to the owner directly; the hirer returns to a place, not to the owner's possession. The hirer's hand after deviation is that of a usurper, and only actual delivery to the owner ends liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical difference: under majority view, compensation is wajib; under Zufar, none. The majority view is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوازه بها عما استؤجر إليه، ولو ذاهباً وجائياً، وردّها إليه
يعني ضمن بهلاكِ الدابة.
[٢] قوله : وجوازه بها ; عطف ثان على قوله : بإرداف رجل ; أي ضمن بجوازه بتلك الدابة عما استؤجر إليه، وصورته : إنّ رجلاً استأجر دابة إلى القادسية مثلاً، فجاوز بها إلى الكوفة مثلاً، ثمّ ردّها إلى القادسية، ثمّ هلكت فيضمن قيمتها ; لأنه صار غاصباً، ولا يبرأ عن الضمان بردها إلى ما سمّاه وهو القادسية.
وقال زفر : لا يضمن ; لأنه لما عاد إلى الوفاق برأ عن الضمان: كالمودع. ولنا : إنَّ يد المستأجر ليست يد المالك ، ولا بد من الرد إليه بعد التعدي وبالعود
And his crossing with it beyond what it was hired for, even if going and returning, and returning it to him means liability for the perishing of the animal. [2] His saying: 'and his crossing with it' is a second conjunct to his saying: 'by giving a man a ride behind him'; that is, he is liable by his crossing with that animal beyond what it was hired for. Its picture: A man hires an animal to go to Qadisiyya, for example, and he crosses with it to Kufa, for example, then returns it to Qadisiyya, then it perishes, so he is liable for its value; because he becomes a usurper, and he is not absolved of liability by returning it to what he named, which is Qadisiyya. Zufar said: He is not liable; because when he returns to conformity, he is absolved of liability, like the depositary. Our proof: The hand of the hirer is not the hand of the owner, and it is necessary to return to him after transgression, and with the return...
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling from Al-Wiqayah about a hirer who takes the animal beyond the agreed destination and then returns it to the destination. The commentator gives an example: a man hires an animal to go to Qadisiyya, but first goes to Kufa, then returns to Qadisiyya, after which the animal dies. The Hanafi position is that the hirer is liable for the full value because, by deviating, he became a usurper (ghasib). Returning to the agreed place does not restore his permission, since the animal was not returned to the owner's possession. Zufar disagrees, analogizing to a depositary who returns a deposit and is exonerated. The Hanafi response is that the hirer's hand is not the owner's hand, and full discharge requires actual delivery to the owner. This highlights the strictness of liability once a contract is breached.

Key Terms
الجواز (al-jawaz): Crossing beyond, exceeding the agreed destination.
الغاصب (al-ghasib): Usurper, one who illegally takes possession of another's property.
المودع (al-mudi'): Depositary, one who receives an item as a trust (wadi'a).
الرد (ar-radd): Returning the animal to a location, here the agreed destination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the hirer still liable after returning the animal to the agreed destination?
A) Because the animal perished before return.
B) Because the hirer became a usurper and return to the place does not equate to return to the owner.
C) Because Zufar argued against liability.
D) Because the animal was not returned in good condition.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a hirer who deviates becomes a usurper and remains liable even after returning to the agreed destination, because liability is only discharged by returning the property to the owner's possession.

Liability for Customary Hitting of Hired Animal
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for hitting or pulling rein of hired animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for customary hitting/pulling of a hired animal and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Customary acts of control in animal hire are implicitly permitted; only unusual acts trigger liability.

When you hire an animal for transport, you may need to control it by hitting or pulling the rein. If such an act causes the animal's death, are you liable? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This passage presents the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and clarifies the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is kabh al-lijam (pulling the rein) or hitting the animal. The dispute: is the hirer liable for the full value if the act causes death?
2
State each position
Abu Hanifa: liable in all cases. Sahibayn: liable only if the act is unusual (ghayr muta'arraf); no liability if customary.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
They argue that custom ('urf) gives implicit permission because animals often require such handling. Custom is like a stipulation (al-ma'ruf 'urf ka al-mashrut shartan), so the contract permits it.
The contract's absolute permission is restricted by custom.
4
Follow Abu Hanifa's proof
He argues that permission is conditional on safety; since driving can be done without hitting/pulling, the contract does not permit such acts. They are excess (al-balaghah fi al-sayr).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage reports that Abu Hanifa may have reverted to the Sahibayn's view, but al-Shami limits this reversion to another issue. The matn authors follow the Sahibayn. Therefore, the mufta bihi is: no liability for customary acts.
The tarjih authority is Allamah al-Shami and the practice of the matn writers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكبح اللجام: جذبُهُ إلى نفسه عنفاً، بسبب الضرب أو كبح اللجام كل قيمتها عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا، إلا أن يكون ضربا أو كبحا غير متعارف. قوله : وعندهما لا...الخ؛ يعني وعندهما لا يضمن إذا فعل فعلاً معتاداً متعارفاً، أما في غير المتعارف فيجب الضمان بالإجماع؛ لأن الكبح المتعارف أو الضرب المتعارف مما يدخل تحت مطلق العقد؛ لكون المعروف عرفاً بمنزلة المشروط شرطاً، وربما لا تكون الدابة منقادة إلا بالضرب أو الكبح فثبت الإذن بالعرف فلا يضمنه. وللإمام: إن الإذن مقيد بشرط السلامة إذ السوق يتحقق بدون الضرب، والكبح وهما للبالغة في السير، فيتقيَّدُ الإذن بوصف السلامة، وذكر في «غاية البيان» نقلاً عن «التتمة»: إن الأصح رجوع الإمام إلى قولهما. (۲) وقال العلامة الشامي: ظاهرُهُ إنّ رجوعه في مسألة الصغير دون الدابة، وينبغي أن يكون كذلك؛ لأنَّ مسألةَ الدَّابة جرى عليها أصحاب المتون، فلو ثبت رجوع الإمام فيها لما مشوا على خلافه؛ لأنَّ ما رجع عنه المجتهد لم يكن مذهباً له. انتهى.
And pulling the rein: pulling it towards oneself forcefully. Due to hitting or pulling the rein, (the hirer is liable for) its full value according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two (companions) no, unless it is unusual hitting or pulling. (The commentator says: His statement: 'And according to them no...etc.' means: According to them he is not liable if he does a customary, usual act. As for an unusual act, liability is obligatory by consensus because customary pulling or hitting falls under the absolute contract due to the fact that what is known by custom is like a condition that is stipulated. Sometimes the animal is not submissive except by hitting or pulling, so permission is established by custom and he is not liable. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa): Permission is conditioned upon safety, because driving can be realized without hitting and pulling, and both are for excess in movement, so permission is restricted to the attribute of safety. And it is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan, quoting from al-Tatimma, that the correct view is that the Imam (Abu Hanifa) returned to their view. And Allamah al-Shami said: Its apparent meaning is that his return was in the issue of the minor, not the animal, and it should be so because the issue of the animal is followed by the authors of the matn. If the Imam's return were established in it, they would not have proceeded contrary to it, because what the mujtahid retracted is not his madhhab. End.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for causing the death of a hired animal through hitting or pulling the rein (kabh al-lijam). The matn states: according to Abu Hanifa, the hirer is liable for the full value if he hits or pulls the rein, regardless of whether the act was customary or not. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), there is no liability if the act was customary and usual; liability only arises if the act was unusual. The commentator expands: the Sahibayn argue that customary acts are implicitly permitted by the contract because custom has the force of a stipulation. Moreover, animals often require such handling, so permission is established by custom. Abu Hanifa counters that permission is restricted to safe handling; since driving can be done without hitting or pulling, the contract does not permit them. However, the commentator notes a report from Ghayat al-Bayan that Abu Hanifa eventually reverted to the Sahibayn's view, though Allamah al-Shami clarifies that the reversion concerned a different issue (a minor), not the animal case, as the authors of the matn consistently uphold the Sahibayn's position. Thus the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn: no liability for customary acts.

Key Terms
كبح اللجام (kabh al-lijam): Pulling the rein of an animal forcefully to stop or control it.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; used here to determine what acts are implicitly permitted in a lease contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on liability if a hirer hits his hired animal in a manner that is customary (mut'arraf)?
A) He is liable for the full value.
B) He is not liable.
C) He is liable only if the animal dies immediately.
D) He is liable for half the value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon position is that customary acts of hitting or pulling the rein in animal hire do not trigger liability; custom is treated as an implicit contractual condition.

Liability for Exceeding Destination and Returning
Ruling on liability for hired animal when hirer exceeds destination and returns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a hirer exceeds the agreed destination and returns, identify the qiyas used, and apply the exception for deaths certainly unconnected to the deviation.

Core Idea
A hirer who deviates becomes a usurper; returning does not discharge liability unless the death is certainly unconnected.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a nuanced case: a hirer who takes a rented animal beyond the agreed point and then brings it back before it perishes. The question is whether the return restores his status as a trustee. The school rules that deviation makes him a ghasib, and return does not automatically remove liability. We will examine the proof based on the nature of the hirer's hand and the important exception for deaths clearly unrelated to the deviation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act: a man hires an animal to a specific destination (e.g., Qadisiyya), exceeds it (goes to Kufa), then returns it to Qadisiyya, where it perishes. The ruling: he is liable for the full value.
The liability arises from becoming a usurper (ghasib) at the moment of deviation.
2
Analyze the legal status after deviation
The hirer's hand is his own, not the owner's. Even after returning to the agreed place, his hand does not revert to being the owner's hand. Hence, return does not discharge liability.
Contrast with a depositary whose hand is the owner's hand.
3
Examine the qiyas with the depositary
Zufar analogized to a depositary who violates trust then returns: liability ceases. The Hanafi refutation: the depositary holds for custody, so his hand represents the owner's; the hirer holds for his own benefit, so his hand is his own. Therefore, the qiyas fails.
The illa (effective cause) is the nature of the hand: owner's vs. hirer's own.
4
Apply the fatwa exception
If the animal perishes from a cause that is certainly unconnected to the deviation (e.g., lightning strike), then there is no liability. Otherwise, liability stands.
This exception preserves causation as a factor, even though the deviation makes one a guarantor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وجوازه بها؛ عطف ثان على قوله: بإرداف رجل؛ أي ضمن بجوازه بتلك الدابة عما استؤجر إليه، وصورته: إنَّ رجلاً استأجر دابة إلى القادسية مثلاً، فجاوز بها إلى الكوفة مثلاً، ثمَّ ردَّها إلى القادسية، ثمَّ هلكت فيضمن قيمتها؛ لأنه صار غاصباً، ولا يبرأ عن الضمان بردها إلى ما سمَّاه وهو القادسية. · وقال زفر: لا يضمن؛ لأنه لما عاد إلى الوفاق برأ عن الضمان: كالمودع. ولنا: إنَّ يد المستأجر ليست يد المالك، ولا بد من الرد إليه بعد التعدي وبالعود قوله: وردها إليه بالجرّ عطف على جوازها": أي يضمن بجواز الدابة عن موضع استؤجرت إليه، ثمَّ ردَّها إلى ذلك الموضع، وإن كان الاستئجار ذاهباً وجائياً؛ وإنما قال هذا نفياً لما قيل إنه إنما يضمن إذا استأجرها ذاهباً فقط؛ لأن الإجارة قد انتهت بالوصول إلى ذلك الموضع، فيضمن بالجواز عنه، أما إذا استأجرها ذاهباً وجائياً فجاوز عن ذلك الموضع، ثمَّ ردَّها إليه لا يضمن كالمودع إذا خالف ثم عاد إلى الوفاق، لكنَّ الصَّحيحَ الضمان. أقول: إن هلكت الدابةُ في ذلك الموضع بسبب يتيقن بأنه لا مدخل لجوازها عن ذلك الموضع في تحقق ذلك السبب يفتى بعدم الضمان، وإن هلكت بسبب لا يتيقن بذلك، بل يمكن أن يكون له مدخل يفتى بالضمان.
His statement: 'And his exceeding by it' – a second conjunct to his statement: 'by giving a ride to a man'; i.e., he is liable by his exceeding with that animal from what he hired it for. Its scenario: a man hired an animal to al-Qadisiyya, for example, then he exceeded with it to Kufa, for example, then returned it to al-Qadisiyya, then it perished – he is liable for its value because he became a usurper (ghasib), and he is not discharged from liability by returning it to the place he named, which is al-Qadisiyya. And Zufar said: He is not liable because when he returned to compliance he is discharged from liability, like the depositary (mudi'). And our proof: The hand of the hirer is not the hand of the owner, and return to him is necessary after transgression and by return. His statement: 'and returning it' – with jarr, a conjunct to 'his exceeding by it' – i.e., he is liable for exceeding the animal from the place he hired it to, then he returned it to that place, even if the hire was for going and coming. And he said this to negate what was said that he is liable only if he hired it for going only; because the hire ended upon reaching that place, so he is liable for exceeding it. But if he hired it for going and coming, then he exceeded from that place, then returned it to him, he is not liable like the depositary when he violates and then returns to compliance, but the correct (view) is liability. I say: If the animal perishes in that place due to a cause that is certain to have no connection to its exceeding from that place, fatwa is given of no liability; if it perishes due to a cause that is not certain, but may have a connection, fatwa is given of liability.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where a hirer uses the hired animal to travel beyond the agreed destination (tajawuz) and then returns it to the agreed point before it perishes. The ruling: the hirer is liable for the value because he became a usurper (ghasib) at the moment of deviation, and mere return does not remove liability. Zufar's contrary opinion analogizes to a depositary (mudi'): if a depositary violates trust and then returns to compliance, liability ceases. The Hanafi refutation distinguishes: a depositary holds the property on behalf of the owner, so return to compliance restores the owner's constructive possession. A hirer, however, holds for his own benefit; his hand is not the owner's hand. Thus, even after returning, liability remains unless the death is demonstrably unrelated to the deviation. The commentator adds a practical fatwa: if the animal dies from a cause certainly unconnected to the deviation (e.g., a natural disaster), there is no liability; otherwise, liability stands. This distinction preserves the principle that deviation makes one a guarantor, but causation matters for liability.

Key Terms
تجاو (tajawuz): Exceeding the stipulated destination in a hire contract.
مستأجر (musta'jir): Hirer; one who rents an animal for transportation or use.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who takes property without owner's permission, incurring strict liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man hires a donkey to go to City A, but he takes it to City B first, then returns to City A where the donkey dies from lightning strike. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling?
A) He is liable because he deviated, regardless of cause of death.
B) He is not liable because he returned to the agreed place and the death was unrelated to the deviation.
C) He is liable only if he used the donkey in City B.
D) He is not liable because the hire was for a single trip.

Key Takeaway
A hirer who exceeds the agreed destination becomes a ghasib, and returning does not discharge liability unless the death is certainly unconnected to the deviation.

Removing the Saddle Does Not Return the Hired Animal
Ruling with contrast to depositary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that removing a part of a hired item does not constitute a valid return and explain the distinction from the depositary's situation.

Core Idea
Removing a part of the hired item does not discharge liability; only full physical delivery to the owner suffices.

This passage clarifies a specific point in hire (ijara) contracts: if a hirer removes the saddle from a hired donkey, that action does not count as returning the animal to the owner. The commentator contrasts this with the case of a depositary (mudi'), whose restoration of a violated deposit effectively returns possession because his hand is deemed the owner's hand. Understanding this rule is essential for determining when a hirer's liability ends.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
The act is removing the saddle (naz' al-sarj) from a hired donkey.
The saddle is part of the hired item (the donkey with saddle).
2
State the ruling
This act does not count as a valid return (radd) of the animal to the owner.
The ruling is explicitly 'la yakunu raddan' - it is not a return.
3
Contrast with the depositary
If a depositary violates the trust and then restores the item, his return is effective because his hand is considered the owner's hand in custody. The hirer has no such constructive possession.
The key difference: the depositary's hand is amanah (trust) linked to the owner; the hirer's hand is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونزع سرج حمار مکتری لا يكون رداً لها إليه بخلاف المودع، فإنَّ يده يد المالك في الحفظ، فإذا عاد المودع إلى الوفاق عاد إلى يد المالك حكماً. [٤]قوله : ونزع سرج ...الخ؛ بالجرّ عطف ثالث على قوله: بإرداف رجل؛ أي ضمن بنزع سرج ... الخ؛ وتقريره: إنَّ رجلاً لو اكترى حماراً بسرج، فنزع السرج
And removing the saddle of a hired donkey does not constitute returning it to him, unlike the depositary, for his hand is the hand of the owner in custody. So when the depositary returns to compliance, he returns to the owner's hand constructively. [4] His statement: 'And removing the saddle...etc.' – with jarr, a third conjunct to his statement: 'by giving a ride to a man'; i.e., he is liable for removing the saddle...etc. Its explanation: A man hired a donkey with a saddle, and he removed the saddle...
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This short passage presents a rule: removing the saddle (sarj) from a hired donkey does not count as returning the animal to the owner, unlike the case of a depositary (mudi'). The reasoning is that a depositary's hand is the owner's hand in terms of custody; thus, if the depositary violates and then returns to compliance (by restoring the item), he effectively returns it to the owner's constructive possession. However, a hirer's removal of the saddle does not restore possession to the owner because the hirer's hand is not the owner's hand. The commentator then links this to the earlier discussion: the liability for removing the saddle is analogous to the previous cases (e.g., exceeding the route) and is treated as a separate actionable wrong. The text is incomplete, but the key point is that removing a part of the hired item (like the saddle) does not discharge the hirer's liability; instead, it may incur additional liability for the saddle itself.

Key Terms
نزع السرج (naz' al-sarj): Removing the saddle from the hired donkey.
مودع (mudi'): Depositary; one who is entrusted with custody of another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It counts as returning the saddle to the owner, discharging liability for the saddle.
B) It does not count as returning the saddle; the hirer remains responsible for it.
C) It cancels the hire contract entirely.
D) It reduces the hire fee by the value of the saddle.

Key Takeaway
Removing a part of a hired item does not return it; only physical delivery to the owner discharges the hirer's liability.

Liability After Deviation and Return of Hired Animal
Commentary on Al-Hidayah explaining liability after deviation and return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the hirer's liability after deviating and returning the hired animal, and explain why the analogy to a depositary is rejected.

Core Idea
Returning a hired animal after deviation does not absolve liability because the hirer is not a primary trustee.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle point in Ijarah: what happens when the hirer deviates from the agreed route or place, but then returns the animal to the proper place. Some argued that upon return, the hirer is no longer liable, analogizing to a depositary who violates the deposit and then returns to compliance. However, the correct Hanafi position is that the hirer remains liable. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the hirer's deviation from the agreed route/place, followed by returning the animal to that place. The ruling is that the hirer is liable for compensation (daman) if the animal perishes, even after return.
2
Locate the dalil
The text mentions an analogy to a depositary (wadi'ah) but rejects it. The dalil is the nature of the hirer's possession: it is yad al-amanah for use, not for safekeeping. Deviation makes the hirer a guarantor (damin), and return does not restore trust.
The analogy to a depositary is the key point of dispute.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hirer's possession is incidental to use; once the use is over, the hirer is no longer a representative of the owner. Deviation itself creates liability, and returning does not undo that.
This distinguishes the hirer from a depositary, whose primary duty is preservation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars argued that for round-trip hiring, returning the animal ends the contract and removes liability, similar to a depositary. This is the minority view.
The minority view is acknowledged but not adopted as mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The correct position (al-sahih) is that liability remains even after return. However, the commentator adds a nuance: if the animal perishes due to a cause certain to be unrelated to the deviation, then no liability. Otherwise, liability applies.
This nuance is a practical fatwa adjustment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وردها إليه بالجرّ عطف على جوازها " : أي يضمن بجواز الدابة عن موضع استؤجرت إليه، ثمّ ردّها إلى ذلك الموضع، وإن كان الاستئجار ذاهباً وجائياً ؛ وإنما قال هذا نفياً لما قيل إنه إنما يضمن إذا استأجرها ذاهباً فقط ؛ لأن الإجارة قد انتهت بالوصول إلى ذلك الموضع، فيضمن بالجواز عنه ، أما إذا استأجرها ذاهباً وجائياً فجاوز عن ذلك الموضع ، ثمّ ردّها إليه لا يضمن كالمودع إذا خالف ثم عاد إلى الوفاق، لكنَّ الصَّحيحَ الضمان. أقول : إن هلكت الدابةُ في ذلك الموضع بسبب يتيقن بأنه لا مدخل لجوازها عن ذلك الموضع في تحقق ذلك السبب يفتى بعدم الضمان ، وإن هلكت بسبب لا يتيقن بذلك، بل يمكن أن يكون له مدخل يفتى بالضمان.
He said: 'And returning it to it by jar (as a genitive) is a conjunction to its permissibility.' Meaning: He is liable by the animal's deviation from the place to which it was hired, then returning it to that place, even if the hiring was one-way and return. He only said this to negate what was said: that he is only liable if he hired it one-way only, because the contract ends upon arrival at that place, so he is liable for deviation from it. But if he hired it one-way and return, and he deviated from that place then returned it to it, he is not liable like a depositary if he violated and then returned to compliance. But the correct (position) is liability. I say: If the animal perishes in that place due to a cause we are certain has no connection to the deviation from that place, the fatwa is no liability. If it perishes due to a cause not certain of that, but it is possible that the deviation had a part in it, the fatwa is liability.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'and returning it to it' from the matn. The commentator explains that the hirer is liable if the animal deviates from the stipulated place, even if he later returns it. This applies whether the hiring was one-way or round-trip. Some argued that if the hiring is one-way, the contract ends upon arrival, so deviation after arrival triggers liability; but if round-trip, upon return to the place the contract is considered fulfilled and thus no liability, akin to a depositary who violates the deposit and later returns to compliance, after which he is no longer liable. However, the correct position according to the Hidayah is that the hirer remains liable even after returning, because the hirer's duty of care is incidental to use, not primary like a depositary. Once the use is over, the hirer is no longer a representative of the owner, so returning does not restore trust. The commentator adds a practical note: if the animal dies after return due to a cause clearly unrelated to the earlier deviation, then no liability; otherwise, liability.

Key Terms
جواز (jawāz): Deviation of the hired animal from the specified place or route.
مستأجر (musta'jir): The person who hires the animal (lessee/hirer).
مودع (mūdi'): A depositary, one entrusted with property for safekeeping (wadi'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position, what is the ruling on a hirer who deviates from the route and then returns the animal to the agreed place, and the animal later perishes?
A) He is not liable because returning restores the owner's possession.
B) He is liable unless the perishing is clearly unrelated to the deviation.
C) He is always liable even if the perishing is unrelated.
D) He is liable only if the hiring was one-way.

Key Takeaway
A hirer who deviates remains liable even after returning the animal, unless the perishing is demonstrably unconnected to the deviation.

Liability for Removing Saddle from Hired Donkey
Commentary on liability for removing saddle and using pack-saddle on hired donkey.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on liability when a hirer changes a hired donkey's saddle to an ikaf, and apply the ruling to similar cases.

Core Idea
Changing a hired donkey's saddle to a pack-saddle changes the genus of use, incurring full liability (Abu Hanifa), while the Sahibayn limit liability to any increase in value.

When you hire a donkey with a saddle, you are entitled to ride it equipped as agreed. If you remove that saddle and replace it with something else, you may become liable if the animal perishes. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute about whether changing to a pack-saddle (ikaf) is a fundamental change or a minor one. Understanding the reasoning behind each position will sharpen your ability to apply fiqh principles to similar rental scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and issue
The passage discusses a person who hires a donkey with a saddle, then removes that saddle and puts an ikaf (pack-saddle) on it, and the donkey dies. The issue is: is the hirer liable for the full value of the donkey, and why?
The ikaf is used for carrying loads, not for riding.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's ruling and reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds the hirer liable for the full value because the ikaf is a different genus from the saddle – one is for riding, the other for loading. The hirer did not receive any part of the contracted benefit, so he is a transgressor.
The reasoning is based on the difference in purpose (istimal) and effect (athar).
3
Understand the Sahibayn's ruling and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the ikaf is like the saddle; if it is the type normally used for donkeys, there is no liability unless the ikaf is more valuable, in which case only the increase is payable.
They view the ikaf as similar riding equipment, so the hirer has not deviated substantially from the contract.
4
Note the agreed point: mere removal of saddle
All agree that merely removing the saddle (without replacing it) and riding bareback makes the hirer liable, because riding bareback is not what was contracted.
This is emphasized by the commentator and from al-Jawharah.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, full compensation is always due when the ikaf is used; under the Sahibayn, only the extra value (if any) is due. This difference affects how damages are calculated in real cases.
The text does not explicitly give tarjih, but the structure gives primacy to Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة باب ما يجوز من الإجارة وما لا يجوز وإيكافه مطلقاً، وإسراجه بما لا يسرج بمثله دون ما يسرج بمثله وإيكافه مطلقاً ، وإسراحه بما لا يسرجُ بمثلِهِ دونَ ما يسرح بمثله) : أي إن اكترى حماراً مسرجاً فَنَزَعَ السَّرج ، وأوكفَهُ وحمل عليه فهلك ضمن سواء كان فأسرجه بسرج لا يسرج بمثله الحمر أو أو كفه مطلقاً أو نزع الإكاف وأسرجه بسرج لا يسرح بمثله، فعطب ضمن جميع قيمته ؛ لأنَّ الإكاف يستعمل لما لا يستعمل له السرج ، وأثره يخالفه أيضاً. فكان خلافاً إلى خلاف جنس المستحق، فلم يصر مستوفياً شيئاً من المسمّى ، فيضمن الكلّ، وقال أبو يوسف و محمد : إن الإكاف كالسرج، فلا يضمن إذا كان يوكف بمثله الحمر إلا إذا زاد على السرج المنزوع فيضمن الزيادة ؛ لأنه كالسرج، فرضاه به كرضاه بالإكاف. واعلم أن مجرد نزع السرج موجب للضمان، كما أفاده كلام الماتن، وصرح به الحموي وغيره، وقال في «الجوهرة»: لو استأجرها ليركبها بسرج لم يركبها عرياً، وإن استأجرها للركوب لم يجز أن يحمل عليها متاعاً ، ولا يجوز أن يستلقي عليها، ولا يتكئ على ظهرها، بل يكون راكباً على العرف والعادة. انتهى.
Book of Ijarah, Chapter of what is permissible and not permissible in Ijarah. 'And his putting an ikaf (pack-saddle) absolutely, and his saddling it with what is not normally saddled like it, not what is normally saddled like it; and his putting an ikaf absolutely, and his setting it free with what is not normally set free like it, not what is normally set free like it.' Meaning: If a man hires a donkey with a saddle, then removes the saddle and puts an ikaf on it and loads it, and it perishes, he is liable. Whether he then saddled it with a saddle that donkeys are not normally saddled with, or put an ikaf absolutely, or removed the ikaf and saddled it with a saddle that is not normally used for it, and it dies, he is liable for its full value. Because the ikaf is used for what the saddle is not used for, and its effect is different. So it is a change to a different type from what is entitled, so he did not receive anything of the specified (item), so he is liable for the whole. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: The ikaf is like the saddle, so he is not liable if donkeys are normally given ikaf like it, unless he increases (the value) over the removed saddle, then he is liable for the increase. Because it is like a saddle, his consent to it is like his consent to the ikaf. Know that merely removing the saddle necessitates liability, as is indicated by the words of the matn, and stated by al-Hamawi and others. And in al-Jawharah: If he hired it to ride with a saddle, he should not ride it bareback; and if he hired it for riding, he may not load goods on it, nor lie down on it, nor lean on its back, but he rides according to custom and usage. End.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a hirer of a donkey changes its equipment. The matn states that if the hirer puts an ikaf (a pack-saddle) absolutely or uses a saddle that is not suitable, he is liable. The commentator explains that if a donkey is hired with a saddle, and the hirer removes that saddle and replaces it with an ikaf (pack-saddle) or a different saddle not normally used for donkeys, and the animal perishes, he is liable for the full value. This is because the ikaf is a different genus from the saddle; one is for riding, the other for carrying loads. Thus the hirer has not fulfilled the contract and is liable. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) disagree: they consider the ikaf like a saddle, so if it is the type normally used for donkeys, there is no liability unless the ikaf is more valuable than the removed saddle, in which case only the increase is payable. The passage also notes that even the simple act of removing the saddle (without replacing it) makes the hirer liable, as riding bareback is not what was contracted. Additional rules from al-Jawharah clarify that the hirer must use the animal according to custom.

Key Terms
إكاف (ikāf): A pack-saddle for a donkey, used for carrying loads, as opposed to a saddle (sirj) for riding.
سرج (sirj): Saddle for riding, typically placed on a horse or donkey.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage; here, the obligation to pay the full value of the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the hirer liable for the full value of a donkey if he removes the saddle and puts an ikaf (pack-saddle) on it?
A) Because the ikaf is more valuable than the saddle.
B) Because the ikaf is a different genus, used for a different purpose, so the hirer did not receive the contracted benefit.
C) Because removing the saddle is always a transgression regardless of what is put.
D) Because the donkey's owner did not consent to the ikaf.

Key Takeaway
The change from saddle to pack-saddle on a hired donkey is considered a change of genus by Abu Hanifa, leading to full liability, while the Sahibayn see it as a minor change, limiting liability to any increase in value.
